يستعين بعلي ؟! وكيف يستنصر من هو جاد في نصره مشمر ذيله في ذلك؟! وروى عنه له أنه قال : - والله ما قتلت عثمان ولا مالات في قتله - وما روي عن علي ه أنه قال في أمر عثمان: الله قتله وأنا معه - معناه: وأنا مع عثمان وكانت الهاء عائدة إلى عثمان دون الله تعالى، وأخبر أنه يستشهد كما استشهد عثمان رضي الله عنهما. يحققه أنه قال على المنبر : - قتلت يوم قتل الثور الأبيض - وهو مثل مشهور في العرب، أخبر بذلك أنه يستشهد كما استشهد هو، والله الموفق. سبب مقتل عثمان : ثم سبب قتله معروف وهو أنه كان ولى بعض أقاربه مصر فجاء أهل مصر يشكونه إليه فعزله، وولى محمد بن أبي بكر وبعثه، ثم افتعل مروان كتابا كتبه بيده وختمه بخاتم عثمان لما أنه كان بيده من غير علم لعثمان وأرسله إليه على يد راكب جمل، وظُفر به وأنكر الكتاب وقال: فعله مروان، فقال له علي: لقد صدق، فصدقه علي في ذلك وعلم أنه لا يفعل مثل هذا، وكان يعينه وينصره، ولو استغاثه لدفع القوم بالحرب، وكان خرج إلى قرية يوم قتله وأقعد على باب الدار الحسن والحسين يحتطانه، فلما رجع عاتبهما فقالا: نُقبَ عليه ولم نعلم، ولقد ترك محمد بن أبي بكر قصد قتله والإعانة عليه لما قال له: لو كان أبوك في الأحياء لاستحييت منه - أو كما قال، فانصرف ثانيًا عنه، ثم أقدم عليه من لا له في فضل ولا يعد من العلماء ولا من الفضلاء، فقتله ظلما وهو يقرأ من سورة فسيكفيكهم الله وهو السميع العليم، وكان رأى النبي عليه السلام في منامه بارحة ذلك اليوم أنه قال: "لا تفطر حتى تفطر معي" فأصبح صائمًا منتظرا القضاء دافعا عن نفسه الردى بالتحصن بالدار متوكلاً على الله في أمره مفوضا أمره إليه متحرزاً عن إراقة دماء المسلمين حتى قال: لا أريد أن يراق في ولايتي قدر محجم من دم مسلم حين طلب منه الإذن فيه ليدفعوا الغوغاء والسفلة عنه.
He seeks help from Ali?! And how can he seek victory from one who is earnest in his support, rolling up his sleeves for that? And it is narrated from him that he said: 'By Allah, I did not kill Uthman nor did I assist in his killing.' And what is narrated from Ali that he said regarding the matter of Uthman: 'Allah killed him and I am with him' — its meaning: 'And I am with Uthman,' and the pronoun refers to Uthman, not to Allah. And he informed that he would be martyred just as Uthman was martyred, may Allah be pleased with them both. He confirmed it by saying on the pulpit: 'I was killed on the day the white bull was killed' — and it is a well-known proverb among the Arabs; he informed by that that he would be martyred just as he (Uthman) was martyred. And Allah is the Grantor of success. ?

?
Ali knew the truth about the forged letter and publicly supported Uthman. When you see someone being wronged or slandered, do you speak up for them even if it costs you social standing or relationships? Or do you stay silent to protect yourself?
?

Proof That Uthman Was Killed Unjustly
Proof of Uthman's innocence via companion conduct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational evidence that Uthman was killed unjustly, based on the conduct of the senior Companions.

Core Idea
The silence of the virtuous proves Uthman's innocence.

This lesson presents a key argument from classical Sunni theology defending the justice of Uthman's caliphate. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, uses the behavior of the most knowledgeable and righteous Companions as proof that Uthman did not deserve to be killed or deposed. By examining their inaction, we can infer Uthman's innocence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the list of Companions mentioned and the rhetorical question at the end.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument is still complete in its main thrust.
2
Identify the key categories
Notice the groups listed: senior Companions, the ten promised Paradise, the Shura council, Badriyyun, first Muhajirun, and Ansar. Why are these specific groups emphasized?
These are the most credible witnesses in the community.
3
Understand the logical argument
The proof is that if Uthman had truly deserved punishment, these eminent Companions would have acted against him. Since they did not, he was unjustly killed.
This is an argument from the absence of dissent among the reliable.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a question: would Ali, Talha, al-Zubayr, and others have tolerated evil from Uthman? The implied answer strengthens the proof.
This question appeals to the known integrity of those Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على أنه قتل مظلومًا، وأنه لم يكن مستحقا للقتل والخلع أن كبار الصحابة ومن بقي من المبشرين بالجنة ومن أهل الشورى والبدريين والمهاجرين الأولين والأنصار - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين لم يشتغلوا بخلعه ولا أرادوا نزعه ولا حاربوه ولا لاموه على فعل من الأفعال وأمر من الأمور، ولو كان الله استحق ذلك لكان أولى به كبار الصحابة ومن سميناهم لا شذاذ القبائل والغوغاء من الخلق والجهال من الناس الذين لم يكن لهم في العلم نصيب ولا مع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم صحبة، أفترى أن عليّا وطلحة والزبير ومن سواهم من أفاضل الصحابة وعلمائهم وكبار خليقة الله تعالى وخيار البشر كانوا يرون المناكير من عثمان
Then the proof that he was killed unjustly, and that he did not deserve killing or removal, is that the senior Companions and those who remained of the ten promised Paradise, and the people of the Shura council, and the Badriyun, and the first Muhajirun, and the Ansar — may Allah be pleased with them all — did not occupy themselves with removing him, nor did they desire his deposition, nor did they fight him, nor did they blame him for any act or matter. If Allah had deserved that, the senior Companions and those we named would have been more entitled to it, rather than the rabble of the tribes, the mob of the creation, and the ignorant people who have no share in knowledge nor companionship with the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. Do you think that Ali, Talha, al-Zubayr and others among the virtuous Companions, their scholars, the great ones of Allah's creation, and the best of mankind, would see reprehensible things from Uthman (and remain silent)?
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof that Uthman was killed unjustly and did not deserve either killing or deposition. The argument is based on the conduct of the senior Companions. The author lists categories: the senior Companions, the remaining ten promised Paradise, the members of the Shura council (appointed by Umar), the participants of Badr, the first Muhajirun, and the Ansar. None of them engaged in efforts to remove Uthman, nor did they desire his deposition, fight him, or even criticize him for any particular action. The author then makes a logical point: if Uthman had truly deserved such treatment (i.e., being killed or deposed), then these eminent figures would have been the ones to carry it out, not the rabble and ignorant people who had no religious knowledge or companionship with the Prophet. He ends with a rhetorical question: would Ali, Talha, al-Zubayr, and other virtuous Companions have seen evil from Uthman and remained silent? The implication is that since they did not act against him, Uthman must have been innocent. The proof relies on the consensus of the most knowledgeable and righteous Companions in not opposing Uthman.

مظلوم
كبار الصحابة
المبشرين بالجنة
أهل الشورى
البدريين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Uthman was deservingly killed due to his policies.
Correction: The passage argues that the senior Companions would have acted if Uthman had truly deserved punishment; their inaction proves his innocence.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the author argue that Uthman was killed unjustly?
A) Uthman was a close relative of the Prophet.
B) The senior Companions did not seek his removal or criticize him.
C) The killers were not Muslims.
D) Uthman had a dream confirming his martyrdom.

Key Takeaway
The inaction of the most virtuous Companions is strong evidence that Uthman was killed unjustly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim being wronged, do you remain silent like the senior Companions who saw no evil from Uthman, or do you speak out? Why, and what does your silence say about your judgment?
?
This argument proves Uthman's innocence by the inaction of the best of creation. But you are not one of them. How would your actions be different if you were among the Companions? Would you have been part of the rabble who acted without knowledge, or part of the righteous who waited for clarity?
?
The Companions did not act because they saw no evil. But you live in a time where evil is rampant. Are you acting like the rabble who act without knowledge, or are you following the example of the virtuous Companions by choosing when to act and when to remain silent? What specific situation in your life right now demands wisdom from this lesson?

Uthman's Innocence and the Companions' Conduct
Argument for Uthman's unjust killing and defense of the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that Uthman was killed unjustly and how the senior Companions' inaction proves his innocence.

Core Idea
The Companions' unanimous inaction proves Uthman was innocent and undeserving of deposition.

This passage presents a key theological argument from the Maturidi tradition defending Caliph Uthman against accusations of misrule. The author argues that the senior Companions, including Ali and the ten promised Paradise, did not seek to depose Uthman or fight him, which proves he was killed unjustly. The lesson unpacks this evidence and refutes the implication that the Companions were complicit through silence.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative of Uthman's killing
Start by reading the description of Uthman's final moments: his fasting, dream, and refusal to shed Muslim blood. This sets the tone for his innocence.
Notice the emphasis on his piety and reluctance to fight.
2
Identify the evidence from the senior Companions
The main evidence is that the senior Companions did not depose or fight Uthman. Identify all the groups listed: the ten promised Paradise, Badr veterans, early emigrants, Ansar, etc.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on the refutation
The author rejects the idea that the Companions were silent because they approved of wrongdoing. Instead, they were cautious about bloodshed. Ask yourself why this matters for Sunni theology.
The passage defends all senior Companions from blame.
4
Consider the conclusion about the Rawafid
The author ties the rebellion to the Rawafid (extremist Shia) and claims it aims to tarnish Ali's reputation. Note how this sectarian point connects to the defense of the Companions.
This reflects the historical polemic with Shia groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأحياء لاستحييت منه - أو كما قال، فانصرف ثانيًا عنه، ثم أقدم عليه من لا له في فضل ولا يعد من العلماء ولا من الفضلاء، فقتله ظلما وهو يقرأ من سورة فَسَيَكْفِيكَهُمُ اللَّهُ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْعَلِيمُ وكان رأى النبي عليه السلام في منامه بارحة ذلك اليوم أنه قال: "لا تفطر حتى تفطر معي" فأصبح صائمًا منتظرا القضاء دافعا عن نفسه الردى بالتحصن بالدار متوكلاً على الله في أمره مفوضا أمره إليه متحرزاً عن إراقة دماء المسلمين حتى قال: لا أريد أن يراق في ولايتي قدر مِحْجَمٍ من دم مسلم حين طلب منه الإذن فيه ليدفعوا الغوغاء والسفلة عنه. ثم الدليل على أنه قتل مظلومًا، وأنه لم يكن مستحقا للقتل والخلع أن كبار الصحابة ومن بقي من المبشرين بالجنة ومن أهل الشورى والبدريين والمهاجرين الأولين والأنصار - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين لم يشتغلوا بخلعه ولا أرادوا نزعه ولا حاربوه ولا لاموه على فعل من الأفعال وأمر من الأمور، ولو كان الله استحق ذلك لكان أولى به كبار الصحابة ومن سميناهم لا شذاذ القبائل والغوغاء من الخلق والجهال من الناس الذين لم يكن لهم في العلم نصيب ولا مع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم صحبة، أفترى أن عليّا وطلحة والزبير ومن سواهم من أفاضل الصحابة وعلمائهم وكبار خليقة الله تعالى وخيار البشر كانوا يرون المناكير من عثمان وكان يغمضون عنها ويمتنعون عن تغييرها والأمر بما يضادها من المعروف، ويرضون بإمامة من هو مستحق للخلع غير صالح للإمامة، وينقادون لأوامره ونواهيه ولا يتعرضون له في إقامة الصلوات والتحكم في الأموال والدماء والفروج، وبسط اليد في أموال بيت المسلمين وهم يعتقدون أنه غير محق فيما يفعل بل هو ظالم متعد حتى جاء من أهل مصر وأهل العراق من لا سابقة له في الإسلام ولا علم له بشيء من أمور الدين، فغيروا كل منكر وأزالوا عن الإسلام معرة الظالم الجائر المبطل، وأراحوا كبار الصحابة عن شره، وتداركوا ما ضيعه المهاجرون والأنصار من حقوق الدين، وقاموا بنصرة من خذله أولئك من المظلومين وحافظوا ما أهمله أولئك من حدود الشرع؟!!. هذا، والله المحال الظاهر والخطأ البين، ووصف خيار الخلق بغير ما وصفهم الله تعالى به وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يظهر أن بغي جميع الروافض يؤول إلى إلحاق شين ونقص بعلي الله والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن الطعن في خيرة خليقته ونجباء بريته المكرمين بصحبة نبيه، الموفقين للقيام بنصرة ما ارتضاه من الدين لعباده الباذلين مهجهم وأموالهم في ذات الله تعالى.
He said to the living, 'I would have felt ashamed of him' — or as he said; then he turned away from him a second time. Then he who has no merit and is not counted among the scholars or the virtuous came to him and killed him unjustly while he was reciting the verse from Surah Al-Baqarah: 'So Allah will suffice you against them, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Knowing.' And he had seen the Prophet (peace be upon him) in his dream the previous night that he said: 'Do not break your fast until you break it with me.' So he began the day fasting, waiting for the decree, defending himself from destruction by fortifying himself in his house, trusting in Allah in his affair, entrusting his affair to Him, cautious of shedding the blood of Muslims, until he said: 'I do not want any amount of blood of a Muslim to be shed during my governorship, even the size of a cupping glass,' when he was asked for permission to repel the mob and the lowly from him. Then the evidence that he was killed wrongfully and that he did not deserve to be killed or deposed is that the senior Companions, those who remained among the ten promised Paradise, the people of the consultation council, the participants of Badr, the early emigrants, and the Ansar — may Allah be pleased with them all — did not engage in deposing him, nor did they desire his removal, nor did they fight him, nor did they blame him for any action or matter. If Uthman had deserved that, then the senior Companions and those we named would have been more entitled to it — not the rabble of the tribes, the mob of people, and the ignorant among men who had no share in knowledge and no companionship with the Prophet (peace be upon him). Do you suppose that Ali, Talhah, Al-Zubayr, and others among the virtuous Companions and their scholars, the great ones of Allah's creation and the best of mankind, saw reprehensible things from Uthman but winked at them, refrained from changing them and from commanding what opposes them of good, and were content with the imamate of one who deserved deposition and was not fit for imamate, and submitted to his commands and prohibitions without interfering with him regarding offering prayers, controlling wealth, blood, and private parts, and extending his hand over the wealth of the Muslims' treasury, while believing that he was not justified in what he did but rather was an oppressor and a transgressor — until people from Egypt and Iraq came who had no precedent in Islam and no knowledge of anything of religious matters, then they changed every evil and removed from Islam the disgrace of the unjust, tyrannical, false ruler, and relieved the senior Companions from his evil, and made up for what the Emigrants and Ansar had neglected of the rights of the religion, and supported those whom those had abandoned of the oppressed, and preserved what those had neglected of the limits of the Sharia?! This, by Allah, is manifest absurdity and clear error. Describing the best of creation with other than what Allah has described them, and reflecting on this statement, shows that the transgression of all the Rawafid leads to attaching blemish and defect to Ali. Praise be to Allah who protected us from attacking the elite of His creation and the nobles of His creatures who were honored by the companionship of His Prophet, and were granted success in supporting that which He approved of religion for His servants, sacrificing their lives and wealth for the sake of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage presents a defense of Caliph Uthman and refutes the claim that the senior Companions were complicit in his oppression by their inaction. The author argues that Uthman was killed unjustly (mazluman) while he was fasting and reciting the Quran, after having a dream from the Prophet. The key evidence for his innocence is that the senior Companions (including Ali, Talha, Zubayr, the ten promised Paradise, Badr veterans, early emigrants, and Ansar) did not attempt to depose him or fight him. If he had deserved to be killed or deposed, they would have been the first to act, not the ignorant mob from Egypt and Iraq. The author rejects the implication that the Companions saw evil from Uthman and remained silent out of weakness or approval; rather, they were patient and cautious about shedding blood. The passage concludes by condemning the Rawafid (refers to Shia extremists) whose rebellion, according to the author, ultimately aims to tarnish Ali's reputation, and praises Allah for protecting the author from criticizing the Companions.

Key Terms
قتل مظلومًا
صحابة
خلع
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The senior Companions' inaction regarding Uthman means they approved of his alleged misdeeds or that he deserved to be deposed.
Correction: The author argues that the senior Companions did not depose Uthman because he was not deserving of deposition; their caution about bloodshed and respect for the caliphate prevented them from acting, not approval of oppression.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the author presents to prove that Uthman was killed unjustly?
A) The senior Companions did not participate in his deposition or fight him.
B) Uthman was reciting the Quran when he was killed.
C) The killers were from Egypt and Iraq.
D) Uthman had a dream from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The senior Companions' unanimous inaction proves that Uthman was killed unjustly and that he did not deserve deposition, contrary to claims of later rebels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticisms of historical Muslim leaders, do you rush to side with the critics assuming the best of them? How does this passage's defense of the Companions' silence challenge you to reconsider your own quick judgments? What would it take for you to trust the collective wisdom of the early community more than your own hindsight?
?
If you were in the position of one of the senior Companions, would you have remained silent while a leader was killed, even if you believed he was innocent? The passage says they did not even blame Uthman. Can you honestly say you would have that much restraint? What does your answer reveal about your own character?
?
The author says that attacking Uthman ultimately defames Ali. Have you ever spoken ill of a scholar or leader in a way that inadvertently dishonored those who respected them? How can you ensure your critiques are constructive and do not lead to subtle attacks on the righteous?

The Well-Known Virtues of Ali ibn Abi Talib
Opening enumeration of Ali's widely known virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive list of Ali's virtues that are universally acknowledged, forming the basis for discussing his imamate.

Core Idea
Ali's virtues were known to all, setting the stage for his claim to leadership.

The author begins the section on Ali's imamate by emphasizing that Ali's virtues are not hidden from anyone. This establishes a foundation of common knowledge before delving into arguments for his caliphate. The passage lists his lineage, close relationship with the Prophet, upbringing, marriage to Fatimah, knowledge, asceticism, piety, bravery, and more.

The common misunderstanding is that Ali's claim was based solely on kinship. The passage shows that many other virtues support his case.
This guards against a narrow view of his qualifications.

Refutation of Rafidah on the Imamate
The author condemns Rafidi beliefs and actions regarding the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author condemns Rafidi beliefs as contradictory and blasphemous.

Core Idea
Criticizing the Companions leads to defaming the Prophet's family.

In this passage, the author addresses a sect that claims to support the Ahl al-Bayt yet criticizes the Prophet's Companions. He argues that their behavior is inconsistent and ultimately insults the very family they revere. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the contrast between the Rafidah's actions and their later rebellion.
The author uses strong rhetorical language to highlight absurdity.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice how the Rafidah first accept an unjust ruler and obey him, but later some rise up to change wrongs. The author considers this a clear contradiction.
This is central to his condemnation.
3
Understand the consequence
The author concludes that the Rafidah's defamation of the Companions ultimately leads to disparaging the Ahl al-Bayt ('Al Allah'). Reflect on why he sees this as inevitable.
This is the crux of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان يغمضون عنها ويمتنعون عن تغييرها والأمر بما يضادها من المعروف، ويرضون بإمامة من هو مستحق للخلع غير صالح للإمامة، وينقادون لأوامره ونواهيه ولا يتعرضون له في إقامة الصلوات والتحكم في الأموال والدماء والفروج، وبسط اليد في أموال بيت المسلمين وهم يعتقدون أنه غير محق فيما يفعل بل هو ظالم متعد حتى جاء من أهل مصر وأهل العراق من لا سابقة له في الإسلام ولا علم له بشيء من أمور الدين، فغيروا كل منكر وأزالوا عن الإسلام معرة الظالم الجائر المبطل، وأراحوا كبار الصحابة عن شره، وتداركوا ما ضيعه المهاجرون والأنصار من حقوق الدين، وقاموا بنصرة من خذله أولئك من المظلومين وحافظوا ما أهمله أولئك من حدود الشرع؟!!. هذا، والله المحال الظاهر والخطأ البين، ووصف خيار الخلق بغير ما وصفهم الله تعالى به وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يظهر أن بغي جميع الروافض يؤول إلى إلحاق شين ونقص بعلي الله والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن الطعن في خيرة خليقته ونجباء بريته المكرمين بصحبة نبيه، الموفقين للقيام بنصرة ما ارتضاه من الدين لعباده الباذلين مهجهم وأموالهم في ذات الله تعالى.
And they used to turn a blind eye to it and refrain from changing it and from enjoining what opposes it of good, and they were content with the imamate of one who deserved to be deposed and was not fit for the imamate, and they obeyed his commands and prohibitions and did not oppose him in establishing prayers and controlling wealth, blood, and private parts, and extending the hand over the wealth of the Muslims' treasury while believing that he was not justified in what he did but rather was an oppressive transgressor, until there came from the people of Egypt and Iraq those who had no precedence in Islam nor any knowledge of religious matters, so they changed every evil and removed from Islam the disgrace of the unjust, tyrannical, false ruler, and relieved the senior Companions from his evil, and made up for what the Muhajirun and Ansar had neglected of the rights of the religion, and they came to the aid of those who had been let down by those oppressors, and preserved what those had neglected of the legal limits of the Sharia?!! This, by Allah, is manifest absurdity and clear error, and describing the best of creation other than with what Allah the Exalted described them, and by reflecting on this summary it appears that the transgression of all the Rafidah leads to attributing defect and deficiency to the family of Allah? And praise be to Allah who protected us from criticizing the choicest of His creation and the nobles of His creatures, honored by the companionship of His Prophet, those given success to support what He has chosen of the religion for His servants, sacrificing their lives and wealth for the sake of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a polemical condemnation of the Rafidah (a Shi'a sect). The author describes their inconsistent behavior: they accept an unjust ruler who deserves to be deposed, obey him, and refrain from commanding good, yet later some people from Egypt and Iraq (referring to rebels who killed Uthman) rise up to change wrongs. The author calls this a manifest absurdity, because the Rafidah's criticism of the Companions—whom Allah praised—ultimately leads to reviling the family of Allah (likely a reference to the Ahl al-Bayt). The author thanks Allah for protecting him from such attacks. The argument follows the author's line that the Rafidah's stance is self-contradictory and blasphemous.

Misconception: The passage condemns all Muslims who criticize rulers.

What will you say to Allah if He asks you about your attitude towards the Companions on the Day of Judgment?
?
When you hear someone disparage a Companion—whether a friend, family member, or online—do you feel a sense of outrage for the honor of the Prophet’s family, or do you remain silent out of convenience? How can this passage motivate you to uphold the honor of the Companions with wisdom and firmness, starting with your next encounter?

Ali's Virtues and Qualifications
The author enumerates Ali's personal virtues and his own testimony to his war experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive virtues of Ali ibn Abi Talib that establish his superiority and qualification for the caliphate according to the Sunni perspective.

Core Idea
Ali's unmatched merit in lineage, knowledge, piety, and military expertise made him the best candidate for leadership.

The author begins by listing Ali's manifest qualities: his close bond with the Prophet, his knowledge, asceticism, and most notably his legendary courage and strategic acumen. This sets the foundation for the argument that Ali was the most deserving of the caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ali's virtues
Read the passage and note each virtue mentioned: lineage, connection to the Prophet, knowledge, piety, courage, military strategy.
Pay attention to the author's emphasis on qualities known to all.
2
Examine Ali's own testimony
Ali refutes the claim that he lacks war experience. His statement underscores his lifelong involvement in warfare.
The quote is a key refutation of sectarian doubts.
3
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author declares these matters too clear for proof and doubts foolish.
This rhetorical closure strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الكلام في إمامة علي بن أبي طالب رضوان الله عليه]: نقول إن عليا رضي الله عنه ممن لا يخفى على أحد نسبه واختصاصه برسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وتربيته إياه وتزويجه كريمته فاطمة الزهراء رضي الله عنها منه ولا علمه ولا زهده ولا ورعه، فأما شجاعته وبأسه ونجدته وعلمه بتدبير الجيوش وجر العساكر وبصارته بمكائد الحرب وحماية البيضة مما صار هو الله به مثلاً سائرا تتداوله الألسن وتعتقده الأفئدة، والرواية عنه مشهورة أنه قال: إن قريشًا تقول إن ابن أبي طالب رجل شجاع، ولكن لا رأي له في الحرب، الله أمرهم من ذا يكون أبصر بها مني وأشد لها مراسًا، والله لقد نهضت فيها وما بلغت العشرين، وها أنا اليوم وقد أشرفت على الستين، ولكن لا أمرة لمن لا يطاع - وهذا أوضح من أن يشتغل بإثباته، ومن ارتاب في أمر من هذه الأمور فهو الأحمق الذي لا دواء لحمقه. ثم بعد ثبوت هذه الشرائط فقد عقدت له الخلافة وهو يومئذ أفضل خليقة الله تعالى على وجه الأرض وأولاهم بها، ثم المتولي لعقدها له كبار الصحابة وأئمة الخلق وخيار من بقي من الصحابة، فإن من المشهور أن قتلة عثمان له كالغافقي وكتانة بن بشر النجيبي، وسوار بن حمران وعبد الله بن شديد بن ورقاء و عمرو بن الحمق الخزاعيين، وآخرين منهم لما قتلوه قصدوا الاستيلاء على المدينة وهموا بالفتك بأهلها وحلفوا على ذلك للصحابة متى لم يقدموا للنظر في أمرهم ويعقدوا الإمامة لرجل منهم، فأرادت الصحابة حسم مادة الفتنة، وعرض هذا الأمر على علي رضي الله عنه، والتمس منه وآثره المصريون فامتنع عليهم، وأعظم قتل عثمان رضي الله عنه وأنشأ بقول : ولو أن قومي طاوعتني سراتهم أمرتهم أمرًا بذبح الأعاديا
[Discussion on the Imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him]: We say: Indeed Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, is one whose lineage and special connection to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) – his upbringing by him and his marriage to his noble daughter Fatima al-Zahra (may Allah be pleased with her) – his knowledge, his asceticism, and his piety are not hidden from anyone. As for his courage, valor, fortitude, knowledge of military strategy, leading armies, insight into war stratagems, and protecting the community – he has become by Allah a proverbial example circulated by tongues and believed by hearts. It is famously narrated from him that he said: 'Quraysh says that Ibn Abi Talib is a brave man, but he has no opinion in war. By Allah, who is more experienced in war than I and more severe in its practice? By Allah, I engaged in it while I had not yet reached twenty, and today I am approaching sixty, but there is no command for one who is not obeyed.' This is too clear to be occupied with proving it. Whoever doubts any of these matters is a fool with no cure for his foolishness. Then after the confirmation of these conditions, the caliphate was contracted for him while he was at that time the best of Allah's creation on the face of the earth and the most deserving of it. Then those who undertook the contract for him were the senior Companions, the leaders of creation, and the best of those who remained from the Companions. For it is well-known that the murderers of Uthman (such as al-Ghafiqi, Kitana ibn Bashr al-Nujaybi, Suwar ibn Hamran, Abdullah ibn Shaddad ibn Warqa', and Amr ibn al-Humq al-Khuza'i, and others from them) when they killed him, intended to take control of Medina and planned to kill its people, and they swore to the Companions that if they did not come forward to consider their affair and contract the imamate to a man from among them, then the Companions wanted to cut off the root of sedition. So they presented this matter to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and requested him, and the Egyptians preferred him, but he refused them. He considered the killing of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) grave, and he recited the verse: 'If my people's leaders had obeyed me, I would have commanded them a command to slaughter the enemies.'
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Ali ibn Abi Talib's right to the caliphate. The author begins by listing Ali's merits: his close relationship to the Prophet (peace be upon him), his knowledge, asceticism, and especially his military expertise. He quotes Ali's own statement refuting the claim that he lacked strategical acumen in war, asserting his experience from youth to old age. Then the author describes the historical context after the assassination of Caliph Uthman: the murderers threatened Medina and forced the remaining Companions to appoint a caliph. The senior Companions offered the caliphate to Ali, but he initially refused due to the gravity of Uthman's murder, reciting a verse expressing his frustration that his people did not follow his earlier advice. The passage thus establishes that Ali was the most qualified and his caliphate was contracted by the leading Companions, countering Shi'a claims that the caliphate was usurped.

Key Terms
علي بن أبي طالب
شجاعة
علم
زهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim Ali lacked strategic acumen in war.
Correction: Ali himself attests to his extensive experience from youth to old age, and the author deems this clear.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed as a virtue of Ali?
A) Lineage and connection to the Prophet
B) Knowledge and piety
C) Wealth and generosity
D) Courage and military strategy

Key Takeaway
Ali's comprehensive excellence in personal and martial virtues made him unquestionably the most qualified for leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear others downplay the qualifications of someone you look up to, do you rush to defend them or do you silently entertain doubts? Ali's own testimony demands that you confront any hidden skepticism you hold about him. Will you accept his word as he gave it, or will you side with the doubters the author calls 'fools'?
?
The author claims Ali's virtues are 'too clear to be occupied with proving them.' Is there a virtue in your own life that you have neglected to cultivate because you assumed it was obvious or unnecessary? What would it take for you to become like Ali in courage, knowledge, or piety?
?
Ali's testimony about his war experience is a personal, first-hand account. Whose testimony about your character would you be ashamed to hear? Are you living in a way that your own words would vindicate you, or would they reveal a gap between your claim and reality?

The Legitimate Contract of Ali's Caliphate
The author describes the political situation after Uthman's murder and how Ali was offered and eventually accepted the caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical circumstances under which Ali was elected caliph and how the senior Companions ensured legitimacy despite the turmoil.

Core Idea
Ali's caliphate was legitimately contracted by the senior Companions under duress from Uthman's murderers to prevent greater sedition.

Having established Ali's qualifications, the author now narrates the events following Uthman's assassination. He explains that the murderers forced the Companions' hand, but it was the leading figures among the Companions who presented the caliphate to Ali. Ali's initial reluctance and his grief over Uthman's death demonstrate his righteousness.

Guided Path
1
Sequence of events
Identify the sequence: murderers' threat, Companions' response, offer to Ali, Ali's refusal.
Notice the author's emphasis on the senior Companions' role.
2
Analyze the threat
The murderers of Uthman planned to seize Medina and forced the Companions to act.
The author names specific individuals to show historical accuracy.
3
Understand Ali's reluctance
Ali considered Uthman's murder a grave matter and initially refused.
His verse quotation indicates frustration that his earlier advice was not followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الكلام في إمامة علي بن أبي طالب رضوان الله عليه]: نقول إن عليا رضي الله عنه ممن لا يخفى على أحد نسبه واختصاصه برسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وتربيته إياه وتزويجه كريمته فاطمة الزهراء رضي الله عنها منه ولا علمه ولا زهده ولا ورعه، فأما شجاعته وبأسه ونجدته وعلمه بتدبير الجيوش وجر العساكر وبصارته بمكائد الحرب وحماية البيضة مما صار هو الله به مثلاً سائرا تتداوله الألسن وتعتقده الأفئدة، والرواية عنه مشهورة أنه قال: إن قريشًا تقول إن ابن أبي طالب رجل شجاع، ولكن لا رأي له في الحرب، الله أمرهم من ذا يكون أبصر بها مني وأشد لها مراسًا، والله لقد نهضت فيها وما بلغت العشرين، وها أنا اليوم وقد أشرفت على الستين، ولكن لا أمرة لمن لا يطاع - وهذا أوضح من أن يشتغل بإثباته، ومن ارتاب في أمر من هذه الأمور فهو الأحمق الذي لا دواء لحمقه. ثم بعد ثبوت هذه الشرائط فقد عقدت له الخلافة وهو يومئذ أفضل خليقة الله تعالى على وجه الأرض وأولاهم بها، ثم المتولي لعقدها له كبار الصحابة وأئمة الخلق وخيار من بقي من الصحابة، فإن من المشهور أن قتلة عثمان له كالغافقي وكتانة بن بشر النجيبي، وسوار بن حمران وعبد الله بن شديد بن ورقاء و عمرو بن الحمق الخزاعيين، وآخرين منهم لما قتلوه قصدوا الاستيلاء على المدينة وهموا بالفتك بأهلها وحلفوا على ذلك للصحابة متى لم يقدموا للنظر في أمرهم ويعقدوا الإمامة لرجل منهم، فأرادت الصحابة حسم مادة الفتنة، وعرض هذا الأمر على علي رضي الله عنه، والتمس منه وآثره المصريون فامتنع عليهم، وأعظم قتل عثمان رضي الله عنه وأنشأ بقول : ولو أن قومي طاوعتني سراتهم أمرتهم أمرًا بذبح الأعاديا
[Discussion on the Imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him]: We say: Indeed Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, is one whose lineage and special connection to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) – his upbringing by him and his marriage to his noble daughter Fatima al-Zahra (may Allah be pleased with her) – his knowledge, his asceticism, and his piety are not hidden from anyone. As for his courage, valor, fortitude, knowledge of military strategy, leading armies, insight into war stratagems, and protecting the community – he has become by Allah a proverbial example circulated by tongues and believed by hearts. It is famously narrated from him that he said: 'Quraysh says that Ibn Abi Talib is a brave man, but he has no opinion in war. By Allah, who is more experienced in war than I and more severe in its practice? By Allah, I engaged in it while I had not yet reached twenty, and today I am approaching sixty, but there is no command for one who is not obeyed.' This is too clear to be occupied with proving it. Whoever doubts any of these matters is a fool with no cure for his foolishness. Then after the confirmation of these conditions, the caliphate was contracted for him while he was at that time the best of Allah's creation on the face of the earth and the most deserving of it. Then those who undertook the contract for him were the senior Companions, the leaders of creation, and the best of those who remained from the Companions. For it is well-known that the murderers of Uthman (such as al-Ghafiqi, Kitana ibn Bashr al-Nujaybi, Suwar ibn Hamran, Abdullah ibn Shaddad ibn Warqa', and Amr ibn al-Humq al-Khuza'i, and others from them) when they killed him, intended to take control of Medina and planned to kill its people, and they swore to the Companions that if they did not come forward to consider their affair and contract the imamate to a man from among them, then the Companions wanted to cut off the root of sedition. So they presented this matter to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and requested him, and the Egyptians preferred him, but he refused them. He considered the killing of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) grave, and he recited the verse: 'If my people's leaders had obeyed me, I would have commanded them a command to slaughter the enemies.'
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Ali ibn Abi Talib's right to the caliphate. The author begins by listing Ali's merits: his close relationship to the Prophet (peace be upon him), his knowledge, asceticism, and especially his military expertise. He quotes Ali's own statement refuting the claim that he lacked strategical acumen in war, asserting his experience from youth to old age. Then the author describes the historical context after the assassination of Caliph Uthman: the murderers threatened Medina and forced the remaining Companions to appoint a caliph. The senior Companions offered the caliphate to Ali, but he initially refused due to the gravity of Uthman's murder, reciting a verse expressing his frustration that his people did not follow his earlier advice. The passage thus establishes that Ali was the most qualified and his caliphate was contracted by the leading Companions, countering Shi'a claims that the caliphate was usurped.

Key Terms
عثمان
قتلة عثمان
الصحابة
الخلافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim Ali sought the caliphate after Uthman's murder.
Correction: The passage shows Ali initially refused and only accepted to prevent greater sedition, showing his reluctance and righteousness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the senior Companions offer the caliphate to Ali after Uthman's murder?
A) Because Ali demanded it
B) To cut off the root of sedition threatened by the murderers
C) Because Uthman had willed it
D) Because the Egyptians forced them

Key Takeaway
Ali's caliphate was legitimate, contracted by senior Companions in a time of crisis, and he accepted only reluctantly to prevent further bloodshed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are thrust into a responsibility you did not seek, do you accept it for the greater good, even if it means personal sacrifice? Ali reluctantly accepted to prevent sedition. What would you have done in his place?
?
The senior Companions acted under intense pressure from murderers. In your life, when have you made a difficult decision under external pressure? Did you prioritize communal well-being over your own comfort, as they did?
?
Ali initially refused the caliphate because of the gravity of Uthman's murder. How do you react when asked to lead in the aftermath of injustice? Are you willing to bear the burden of healing a broken situation?

The Pledge to Ali and the Validity of Caliphate Without Consensus
Detailing the pledge to Ali and the rule that unanimous consensus is not required for caliphate validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical circumstances of Ali's pledge of allegiance after Uthman's murder and the legal principle that the validity of the caliphate does not require unanimous consensus but rather a sufficient group of qualified Muslims.

Core Idea
Ali's caliphate was valid even without universal consensus; a sufficient pledge suffices.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla narrates the events leading to Ali's acceptance of the caliphate after Uthman's murder. It also establishes a key legal principle: the validity of the caliphate does not depend on unanimous consensus (ijma') but on a sufficient number of qualified Muslims pledging allegiance. Understanding this helps clarify objections about the legitimacy of Ali's reign.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical narrative
Read the first part of the passage describing the pressure on Ali to accept the caliphate after the murder of Uthman and the refusal of Talha and Zubayr. Note the atmosphere of fitna.
2
Identify the Companions who pledged
Find the list of prominent Companions who gave bay'ah to Ali. Recognize that they represent a sufficient group.
3
Grasp the legal principle
Focus on the final sentence: the author asserts that full consensus is not a condition for caliphate validity. Understand that this refutes the objection that Ali's caliphate was invalid.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this principle applies to later discussions of caliphate legitimacy and the role of consensus in Sunni political thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عرض على الزبير فامتنع أيضا كل ذلك إنكارا منهم وإعظامًا، فلما حلف أهل الفتنة على الفتك بأهل المدينة والقاح الفتنة بها اجتمع وجوه المهاجرين والأنصار - رضي الله عنهم - من عشية اليوم الثالث - على ما روي - من قتل عثمان فسألوا عليا لله ولعنه هذا الأمر وأقسموا عليه فيه، وناشدوه الله في حفظ بقية الأمانة وصيانة دار الهجرة، فدخل في ذلك بعد شدة وبعد أن رأه مصلحة ورأى القوم ذلك بعلمهم وعلمه أنه أعلم من بقى من الصحابة وأفضلهم وأولاهم به فمد يده، وبايعه جماعة ممن حضر منهم: خزيمة بن ثابت، وأبو الهيثم بن التيهان ومحمد بن مسلمة، وعمار وأبو موسى الأشعري، وعبد الله بن عباس - رضي الله عنهم - في رجال يكثر عددهم. وقد بينا أن ليس من شرط صحة الخلافة انعقاد الإجماع، بل متى عقد بعض
Then it was presented to al-Zubayr, and he also refused, all of them denouncing and magnifying [the event]. Then when the people of discord swore to harm the people of Medina and to ignite fitna there, the leaders of the Muhajirun and Ansar gathered on the evening of the third day—as narrated—from the killing of Uthman, and they asked Ali for the sake of God and for the sake of the Uthman incident to accept this matter, and they adjured him to preserve the remainder of the trust and protect the abode of the Hijra. So he entered into it after hardship and after he saw it as a benefit, and the people saw that with their knowledge, and he knew that he was the most knowledgeable of the remaining Companions, the most virtuous, and the most deserving of it. So he extended his hand, and a group of those present pledged allegiance to him: Khuzayma ibn Thabit, Abu al-Haytham ibn al-Tayhan, Muhammad ibn Maslama, Ammar, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and Abdullah ibn Abbas—may God be pleased with them—along with many others. And we have shown that the validity of the caliphate does not require the occurrence of consensus, but rather when some [sufficient number] contract it ...
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage continues the narrative and includes a key legal principle. After Talha and Zubair also refused the caliphate, the situation in Medina became dire: the murderers of Uthman threatened violence. To prevent fitna, the leading Companions from both Muhajirun and Ansar gathered and insisted that Ali accept the caliphate. The author lists the prominent Companions who pledged allegiance to Ali, including Khuzayma ibn Thabit, Abu al-Haytham ibn al-Tayhan, Muhammad ibn Maslama, Ammar ibn Yasir, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and Abdullah ibn Abbas. The passage then states a crucial legal condition: that the validity of the caliphate does not require unanimous consensus (ijma') of all Muslims. Rather, it is valid if a sufficient group (some of those qualified) contract it. This directly refutes objections that Ali's caliphate was invalid because not all Companions agreed. The text cuts off, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many believe that the caliphate is only valid if every single Companion (or all Muslims) agree on the caliph.
Correction: The author states explicitly that the validity of the caliphate does not require universal consensus; rather, it is valid when a sufficient group of qualified Muslims contract it.

Checkpoint
What condition for the validity of the caliphate does the author assert at the end of this passage?
A) It requires the approval of all Muslims.
B) It does not require universal consensus; only a sufficient group is needed.
C) It requires the consent of the majority of the Quraysh.
D) It requires the presence of all the Companions of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The validity of the caliphate rests on a sufficient pledge from qualified Muslims, not on universal consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage emphasizes that the most knowledgeable and virtuous Companion, Ali, was chosen. Do you actively seek out knowledge and virtue to become a leader worthy of trust? In what specific area can you commit to growth starting today?

Ali's Pledge and the Principle of Valid Imamate
Nasafi recounts Ali's election and states imamate does not require consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Imam Nasafi, the validity of the caliphate does not require universal consensus but rather a pledge from a sufficient number of qualified people.

Core Idea
Valid imamate needs qualified people, not unanimous consensus.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla combines a historical account of Ali's accession with a key Maturidi principle on political legitimacy. Nasafi recounts how after Uthman's assassination, the leading Companions eventually persuaded Ali to accept the caliphate despite initial reluctance. The doctrinal takeaway is that the imamate is valid when righteous people pledge to a qualified candidate—it does not need universal approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice both the historical narrative and the doctrinal statement at the end.
The passage has two parts: the story of Ali's election and the ruling on imamate.
2
Identify the key events
Note that Talha and Zubayr initially refused the caliphate. After sedition escalated, the Muhajirun and Ansar urged Ali to accept. He agreed after seeing it as a benefit.
3
List the Companions who pledged
Find the names of those who pledged: Khuzayma, Abu al-Haytham, Muhammad ibn Maslama, Ammar, Abu Musa, Ibn Abbas, and many others. This shows broad support, not universal.
4
Extract the doctrine
Read the final sentence: 'ليس من شرط صحة الخلافة انعقاد الإجماع، بل متى عقد بعض صالحي الأمة لمن هو صالح لذلك مستجمع للشرائط انعقدت.' This is the core principle.
Nasafi explicitly rejects universal consensus as a condition.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: It defends Ali's caliphate against critics who said it lacked consensus. It also establishes a practical criterion for leadership in Muslim polity.
This is a fundamental Maturidi political doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولزم بيته ثم عرض ذلك على طلحة وآثره البصريون، فأبى ذلك وكرهه وأنشأ يقول: ومن عجب الأيام والدهر أنني بقيت وحيدا لا أمر ولا أحلى لقتل عثمان ثم عرض على الزبير فامتنع أيضا كل ذلك إنكارا منهم وإعظاما، فلما حلف أهل الفتنة على الفتك بأهل المدينة والقاح الفتنة بها اجتمع وجوه المهاجرين والأنصار - رضي الله عنهم - من عشية اليوم الثالث - على ما روي - من قتل عثمان فسألوا عليا لله ولعنه هذا الأمر وأقسموا عليه فيه، وناشدوه الله في حفظ بقية الأمانة وصيانة دار الهجرة، فدخل في ذلك بعد شدة وبعد أن رأه مصلحة ورأى القوم ذلك بعلمهم وعلمه أنه أعلم من بقى من الصحابة وأفضلهم وأولاهم به فمد يده، وبايعه جماعة ممن حضر منهم: خزيمة بن ثابت، وأبو الهيثم بن التيهان ومحمد بن مسلمة، وعمار وأبو موسى الأشعري، وعبد الله بن عباس - رضي الله عنهم - في رجال يكثر عددهم. وقد بينا أن ليس من شرط صحة الخلافة انعقاد الإجماع، بل متى عقد بعض صالحي الأمة لمن هو صالح لذلك مستجمع للشرائط انعقدت.
He stayed in his house, then presented that matter to Talha, and the Basrans followed him (or: he preferred him?), but he refused and disliked it and recited: 'And from the wonders of days and time is that I remain alone, I neither command nor am satisfied' regarding the killing of Uthman. Then he presented it to al-Zubayr, and he also refrained, all of that out of denial and veneration of the matter. When the people of sedition swore to kill the people of Medina and to kindle sedition there, the leading figures of the Muhajirun and Ansar - may Allah be pleased with them - gathered on the evening of the third day - according to what is narrated - from the killing of Uthman, and they asked Ali, for Allah and His curse, this matter, and they adjured him concerning it, and they implored him by Allah to preserve the remainder of the trust and protect the abode of emigration. So he entered into it after hardship and after he saw it as a benefit, and the people saw that with their knowledge, and he knew that he was the most knowledgeable of the remaining Companions and the most virtuous and the most entitled to it. So he extended his hand, and a group of those present pledged to him, among them: Khuzayma ibn Thabit, Abu al-Haytham ibn al-Tayyihan, Muhammad ibn Maslama, Ammar, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and Abdullah ibn Abbas - may Allah be pleased with them - and many other men. And we have explained that the condition for the validity of the caliphate is not the enactment of consensus, but rather when some of the righteous people pledge to someone who is qualified for it, possessing the conditions, it is valid.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage combines a historical narration with a doctrinal principle. First, Nasafi recounts the events after Uthman's murder: Ali stayed home, Talha and Zubayr initially refused the caliphate, and after the sedition escalated, the leading Companions implored Ali to accept. He agreed after seeing it as a public benefit and knowing his own merit. Then Nasafi states the key doctrinal point: the validity of the caliphate does not depend on universal consensus (ijma'). Rather, it is valid when a sufficient number of righteous people (sulaha' al-ummah) pledge to a qualified person. This is a fundamental Maturidi principle on the legitimacy of political leadership, defending Ali's caliphate against objections that it lacked universal support.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Sunni theology requires unanimous consensus of all Muslims for a caliph to be legitimate.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that the condition for valid caliphate is not universal consensus, but a pledge from a sufficient number of righteous people to a qualified candidate.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the condition for the validity of the caliphate?
A) Universal consensus (ijma') of all Muslims
B) Pledge of a sufficient number of righteous people to a qualified person
C) Appointment by the previous caliph
D) Hereditary succession

Key Takeaway
The validity of the caliphate rests on a pledge by qualified people, not universal consensus, as demonstrated in Ali's election.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take for you to pause and measure your motives before accepting a position of authority?
?
Nasafi clarifies that valid leadership does not require everyone's approval. Whose approval are you seeking that holds you back from doing what is right? Will you today act on a decision you know is correct even if others disagree?
?
The righteous people (sulaha') pledged to Ali despite the chaos. Are you surrounding yourself with righteous companions who will support you in difficult decisions? If not, what step will you take this week to cultivate such relationships?

When a Pledge is Forced
Nasafi answers the objection that Talha and Zubayr's forced pledge invalidates Ali's imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Nasafi, a forced pledge from even prominent companions does not invalidate the caliphate.

Core Idea
A coerced pledge does not break the caliphate.

We just learned that the caliphate does not require universal consensus. Now an opponent objects: what if Talha and Zubayr only pledged under duress? Nasafi’s reply is concise and powerful. Let’s see how he turns the objection on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice that the objection includes a quote from Talha and Zubayr, and Nasafi’s answer is immediate.
2
Identify the objection
What is the central claim? That forced consent invalidates the pledge, and therefore Ali’s imamate is unsound.
The objectors cite Talha and Zubayr saying their hearts did not pledge.
3
Grasp Nasafi’s answer
Nasafi dismisses the whole line of argument. The imamate was valid even without their pledge. He refers back to the earlier principle that the caliphate does not require every individual’s free consent.
The word 'this' (هذا) ties back to that established rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجاب عن قول من يقول : إن طلحة والزبير بايعاه كرها وقالا بايعناه أيدينا ولم تبايعه قلوبنا - أن إمامته بدون بيعتهما كانت صحيحة.
And by this is answered the statement of those who say: Talha and al-Zubayr pledged to him unwillingly and said, 'We pledged to him with our hands, but our hearts did not pledge to him' - that his imamate was valid without their pledge.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that Talha and Zubayr pledged to Ali under coercion, and even they admitted their hearts did not consent. This might suggest the pledge was invalid. Nasafi's answer is concise: the imamate was valid without their pledge. The 'this' (هذا) refers back to the earlier principle that the caliphate does not require universal consensus. Even if their pledge was forced, it does not affect the overall validity of Ali's imamate, because it was already established by the pledge of others.

Key Terms
بيعة
كرها
إمامة
صحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person or group pledges under duress, their pledge is invalid, and this makes the entire caliphate of the leader invalid.
Correction: Nasafi shows that even if the pledge of Talha and al-Zubayr was forced, the imamate remains valid because it does not depend on every individual’s free consent; it is established by sufficient legitimate pledges.

Checkpoint
What is Nasafi's basis for answering the objection about Talha and Zubayr's forced pledge?
A) The pledge was not actually forced
B) Talha and Zubayr later confirmed their pledge
C) The imamate was valid even without their pledge
D) Ali did not accept their pledge

Key Takeaway
The imamate does not collapse because some pledges are coerced; its validity rests on sufficient legitimate consent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever obeyed a command from a leader or parent outwardly while your heart was reluctant? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the validity of your obedience in Allah's sight? Will you examine your intentions today and seek to align your heart with your actions?
?
The companions' hands pledged but their hearts did not. In what areas of your life do you go through the motions—prayer, charity, or other duties—without full inner consent? What step can you take this week to bring your heart into alignment with your outward submission?
?
If the imamate stands despite some forced pledges, what does that teach you about supporting legitimate authority even when you disagree? Is there a leader or decision you have resisted obeying? Will you commit to fulfilling your duty outwardly while asking Allah to soften your heart?

Why Some Companions Refrained from Supporting Ali
Nasafi answers that many companions refrained from fighting, but did not reject Ali's imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain distinguished Companions refrained from supporting Ali militarily and how their actions do not undermine the legitimacy of his imamate.

Core Idea
Refraining from fighting can be a religious duty, not a rejection of authority.

This passage addresses a common objection raised against the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate: that many notable Companions did not fight alongside him. Nasafi explains that their non-participation was not a rejection of his imamate but rather a conscientious decision based on prophetic warnings against intra-Muslim conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument. Notice the specific Companions mentioned and the flow of reasoning.
The passage contains both the objection and Nasafi's multi-part answer.
2
Identify the core points of the answer
Look for each reason Nasafi gives to justify the Companions' choice. Note how he separates the validity of the imamate from the obligation to support him in battle.
Pay attention to the hadith cited by Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas.
3
Consider the misconception
Reflect on the common misunderstanding that not fighting implies rejection. See how Nasafi uses hadith evidence to show it is the opposite.
The misconception is addressed directly in the common_misunderstanding field.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون أيضا عن قولهم: إن سعد بن أبي وقاص، وسعد بن زيد بن عمرو بن نفيل، وأسامة بن زيد، وغيرهم ممن يكثر عددهم قعدوا عن نصرته والدخول في طاعته، فإن إمامته انعقدت صحيحة بدون بيعة هؤلاء، على أنه لم يكن من هؤلاء أحد طعن في إمامته، ولا اعتقد فسادها بل قعدوا عن نصرته على حرب المسلمين حتى قال واحد منهم : لا أقاتل حتى يأتيني سيف له لسان يعرف المؤمن من الكافر يقول: هذا مؤمن فلا تقتله، وهذا كافر فاقتله - ولم يقل إنك لست بإمام واجب الطاعة، وقال محمد بن مسلمة رضي الله عنه بعد مراجعة ومفاوضة : إن رسول الله عهد إلي إذا وقعت الفتنة أن أكسر سيفي وأتخذ مكانه سيفا من خشب، ثم إنهم لم يأثموا بتركهم نصرته وإن كان هو إماما، لأنه لم يدعهم إلى الحرب ولم يلزمهم ذلك بل تركهم وما اختاروا، وكان اختيارهم ذلك بناء على أحاديث رووها عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فإن سعد بن أبي وقاص رضي الله عنه قال: قتال المسلم كفر وسبابه فسوق، ولا يحل لمسلم أن يهجر أخاه فوق ثلاثة أيام.
And by this they are also answered regarding their statement: Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'd ibn Zayd ibn Amr ibn Nufayl, Usama ibn Zayd, and others too numerous to count refrained from supporting him and entering into his obedience - indeed his imamate was validly established without the pledge of these, and not one of these disparaged his imamate, nor believed in its corruption; rather they refrained from supporting him in fighting the Muslims, until one of them said: 'I will not fight until a sword comes to me that has a tongue which knows the believer from the disbeliever, saying: This is a believer, do not kill him; and this is a disbeliever, kill him' - and he did not say: 'You are not a leader whose obedience is obligatory.' And Muhammad ibn Maslama, may Allah be pleased with him, said after consultation and discussion: 'The Messenger of Allah entrusted me that when sedition occurs I should break my sword and take a sword of wood in its place.' Then they did not sin by leaving his support, although he was an imam, because he did not call them to war nor obligate that upon them, but left them to their choice. And their choice was based on hadiths they narrated from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. For Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Fighting a Muslim is disbelief (kufr) and insulting him is transgression (fisq), and it is not permissible for a Muslim to abandon his brother for more than three days.'
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objection is that many notable Companions (like Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Usama, etc.) did not support Ali militarily, which might cast doubt on the legitimacy of his imamate. Nasafi gives a multi-part answer. First, the imamate was validly established without their pledge, so their absence does not harm its validity. Second, they never questioned Ali's imamate or considered it corrupt; they simply refrained from fighting. Third, their refusal was based on prophetic hadiths warning against intra-Muslim fighting, and they sought clear signs to distinguish believers from disbelievers. Fourth, Muhammad ibn Maslama explicitly cited a prophetic instruction to avoid fighting in times of fitna. Fifth, they had the freedom to choose because Ali did not compel them. Finally, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas is quoted as saying fighting a Muslim is kufr. Thus, their non-participation was a religiously motivated choice, not a rejection of Ali's authority.

Key Terms
نصرة
طاعة
بيعة
فتنة
قتال المسلم كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that not fighting for the caliph implies rejecting his authority.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that it can be based on legitimate religious reasons, especially to avoid fitna.

Checkpoint
Why did Companions like Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas refrain from supporting Ali in battle?
A) They rejected Ali's caliphate
B) They were forced to remain neutral
C) They relied on hadiths forbidding fighting among Muslims
D) They were waiting for a sign from heaven

Key Takeaway
Not fighting for the imam does not necessarily imply rejection of his authority; it can be based on legitimate religious concerns, especially to avoid fitna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you stayed neutral in a conflict because you feared it would cause division? Did you justify it by religious reasons, or was it just convenience? How does Nasafi's argument challenge you to examine your motives?
?
If a leader you respect calls you to war, how do you decide whether to obey? Can you cite a hadith to excuse yourself? Are you prepared to stand before Allah for that choice?
?
The Companions had authentic hadiths. Do you have the same certainty? Or do you choose your allegiances based on convenience? What would it take for you to break your sword?

The Validity of Imamah Without Reluctant Pledges
Answering objection about Talha and Zubayr's forced allegiance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the validity of 'Ali's imamah did not depend on the willing pledge of Talha and Zubayr, and that their reluctance did not invalidate his leadership.

Core Idea
Imamah is valid by contract, not by the sincerity of every pledge.

The first objection to 'Ali's caliphate involves claims that Talha and Zubayr pledged allegiance unwillingly. This passage demonstrates that such reluctance does not affect the moral and legal validity of the imamah, as the contract of bay'ah is based on outward action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Talha and Zubayr
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim and the response: their reluctance does not invalidate 'Ali's imamah.
Focus on the phrase 'that his imamah was valid without their pledge'.
2
Understand the principle
The passage establishes that imamah is valid once established by the people of power and influence; individual reluctance or insincere pledge does not nullify it.
This is a key principle in Sunni political theology.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this principle protects the legitimacy of a leader even when some supporters are unwilling. How does this relate to the broader context of fitna?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجاب عن قول من يقول : إن طلحة والزبير بايعاه كرها وقالا بايعناه أيدينا ولم تبايعه قلوبنا - أن إمامته بدون بيعتهما كانت صحيحة. وبهذا يجابون أيضا عن قولهم: إن سعد بن أبي وقاص، وسعد بن زيد بن عمرو بن نفيل، وأسامة بن زيد، وغيرهم ممن يكثر عددهم قعدوا عن نصرته والدخول في طاعته، فإن إمامته انعقدت صحيحة بدون بيعة هؤلاء، على أنه لم يكن من هؤلاء أحد طعن في إمامته، ولا اعتقد فسادها بل قعدوا عن نصرته على حرب المسلمين حتى قال واحد منهم : لا أقاتل حتى يأتيني سيف له لسان يعرف المؤمن من الكافر يقول: هذا مؤمن فلا تقتله، وهذا كافر فاقتله - ولم يقل إنك لست بإمام واجب الطاعة، وقال محمد بن مسلمة له بعد مراجعة ومفاوضة : إن رسول الله عهد إلى إذا وقعت الفتنة أن أكسر سيفي وأتخذ مكانه سيفا من خشب، ثم إنهم لم يأثموا بتركهم نصرته وإن كان هو إمامًا، لأنه لم يدعهم إلى الحرب ولم يلزمهم ذلك بل تركهم وما اختاروا، وكان اختيارهم ذلك بناء على أحاديث رووها عن رسول الله عليه السلام فإن سعد بن أبي وقاص روى عنه قال : قتال المسلم كفر وسبابه فسوق، ولا يحل لمسلم أن يهجر أخاه فوق ثلاثة أيام. ويروي هو أيضا عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال: "ستكون بعدي فتنة القاعد فيها خير من القائم والقائم فيها خير من الماشي والماشي فيها خير من الساعي." قال: وأراه قال - والمضطجع فيها خير من القاعد فقعدوا عن نصرته متأولين لهذه الأحاديث فتركهم وما اختاروا هم لأنفسهم، فلم يقدح ذلك في إمامته، ولا كانوا هم بذلك مرتكبين مأثم. يحققه: أن عليا خطب بعد أمر الحكمين خطبة قال فيها بعد كلام طويل: الله منزل نزله سعد بن مالك وعبد الله بن عمرو، والله لئن كان ذنبًا إنه لصغير مغفور، وإن كان حسنًا إنه لعظيم مشكور - وهذا تصريح منه أنه لا يرى تأثيمهم في قعودهم عن نصرته.
And with this, an answer is given to the statement of those who say: 'Talha and al-Zubayr pledged allegiance to him unwillingly, and said: we pledged allegiance with our hands but our hearts did not pledge allegiance' — that his imamah (leadership) was valid without their pledge of allegiance. And with this, they are also answered regarding their statement: 'Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'd ibn Zayd ibn 'Amr ibn Nufayl, Usama ibn Zayd, and others whose number is many, refrained from supporting him and entering into obedience to him.' For his imamah was validly established without the pledge of these ones, despite the fact that none of these ones impugned his imamah, nor believed in its corruption; rather, they refrained from supporting him regarding the fighting against the Muslims, until one of them said: 'I will not fight until a sword comes to me that has a tongue which recognizes the believer from the disbeliever, saying: this one is a believer, so do not kill him, and this one is a disbeliever, so kill him' — and he did not say: 'You are not an imam whose obedience is obligatory.' And Muhammad ibn Maslamah said to him after consultation and discussion: 'Indeed, the Messenger of God entrusted to me that when tribulation occurs, I should break my sword and take in its place a sword of wood.' Then, they did not sin by abandoning his support, even though he was an imam, because he did not call them to war nor oblige them to that; rather, he left them and what they chose. And their choice was based on hadiths they narrated from the Messenger of God (peace be upon him), for Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas narrated from him that he said: 'Fighting a Muslim is disbelief (kufr), and abusing him is transgression (fisq), and it is not permissible for a Muslim to abandon his brother for more than three days.' And he also narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'There will be after me a tribulation (fitna): the one sitting during it is better than the one standing, and the one standing is better than the one walking, and the one walking is better than the one running.' He said: I think he said: 'and the one lying down is better than the one sitting.' So they refrained from supporting him, interpreting these hadiths, and he left them and what they chose for themselves. That did not detract from his imamah, nor did they thereby commit any sin. And he confirms it: that 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) delivered a sermon after the affair of the two arbitrators, in which he said after a long speech: 'God is the one who sent down [the decision] regarding Sa'd ibn Malik and 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr. By God, if it was a sin, it is a small forgiven one, and if it was good, it is great and thankworthy.' And this is an explicit statement from him that he did not consider them sinful for their refraining from supporting him.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage addresses objections regarding the caliphate of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. It begins by answering the claim that Talha and al-Zubayr pledged allegiance reluctantly, saying they pledged with their hands but not their hearts. The response is that 'Ali's imamah was valid without their pledge. Next, it addresses the objection that many Companions like Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'd ibn Zayd, and Usama ibn Zayd refrained from supporting 'Ali in battle. The answer states that their lack of support did not invalidate his imamah, and they did not dispute his imamah; rather, they refrained from fighting based on interpretations of hadith. One Companion said he would not fight until he had a sword that could distinguish believer from disbeliever. Muhammad ibn Maslamah said the Prophet had instructed him to break his sword in times of fitna. They did not consider 'Ali not an imam. Moreover, 'Ali did not force them to fight. Their choice was based on hadith, such as the one from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas that fighting a Muslim is disbelief and abusing him is sin. Then the passage cites the hadith about the fitna where sitting is better than standing, etc., so they refrained out of interpretation, which did not detract from 'Ali's imamah. Finally, 'Ali himself, in a sermon after the arbitration, stated that if their staying away was a sin, it was a small forgiven one, and if it was good, it was great and thankworthy, indicating he did not consider them sinful.

Key Terms
إمامة (imamah)
بيعة (bay'ah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imamah is invalid if some individuals pledge reluctantly or insincerely.
Correction: Imamah is contractually valid with the pledge of the people of power; individual reluctance does not affect it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of Talha and Zubayr's reluctant pledge on 'Ali's imamah?
A) It invalidates the imamah.
B) It makes the imamah questionable.
C) The imamah remains valid without their pledge.
D) The imamah becomes valid only if they repented.

Key Takeaway
The validity of imamah does not require unanimous enthusiastic support; the outward pledge of the people of authority suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with a decision made by those in authority over you (e.g., a parent, teacher, or community leader), do you withhold your cooperation because your heart isn't fully behind it? How does this lesson challenge your behavior regarding pledges you have made (like bay'ah to a leader or a promise to support a cause)? Are you willing to fulfill your outward commitments even when your feelings lag behind?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from fulfilling a commitment—like a promise to support a community project or a pledge to a leader—because you felt you were forced into it? Does this teaching allow you to use that excuse? What does it require of you instead? Ask yourself: Will I now honor my pledges even if my heart is not fully in agreement, trusting that the validity of the contract does not depend on my inner emotions?

Justification of Companions Who Refrained from Supporting 'Ali
Answering objection about numerous Companions not supporting 'Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that many Companions refrained from supporting 'Ali in battle due to their interpretation of prophetic hadiths about fitna, and 'Ali himself did not consider them sinful.

Core Idea
Interpreting hadiths about fitna can justify withdrawal from battle without sin.

The second objection addresses numerous Companions who did not support 'Ali in the civil war. The passage explains that they acted based on their understanding of hadiths warning about fitna, and 'Ali explicitly stated he did not hold them sinful.

Guided Path
1
Read the section about the Companions
Read the Arabic passage and translation about Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'd ibn Zayd, Usama ibn Zayd, and others. Notice that they did not impugn 'Ali's imamah.
Highlight the phrase 'they did not sin by abandoning his support'.
2
Examine the hadiths they cited
Two hadiths are mentioned: one about fighting a Muslim being kufr, and another about the virtue of sitting during fitna. Understand how these justified their withdrawal.
The hadith about the sword is also symbolic.
3
Understand 'Ali's statement
After the arbitration, 'Ali said that if their staying away was a sin, it was small and forgiven; if good, it was great. This shows he did not condemn them.
This confirms their actions were not sinful in his view.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how legitimate differences in interpretation (ijtihad) can lead to different actions without sin, especially in times of fitna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Companions who did not support 'Ali were sinful or disloyal.
Correction: They acted on valid hadith interpretations, and 'Ali absolved them.

Checkpoint
What did 'Ali say about those who refrained from supporting him, according to the passage?
A) They were sinners.
B) If it was a sin, it was small and forgiven; if good, it was great.
C) They were disbelievers.
D) He expelled them from the community.

Key Takeaway
Companions who refrained from supporting 'Ali did not sin, as they interpreted hadiths about fitna, and 'Ali himself did not condemn them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions who refrained from supporting 'Ali based their decision on their understanding of prophetic hadiths about fitna. When you face a confusing situation where loyalties are divided, do you seek guidance from the Quran and Sunnah with sincere ijtihad, or do you simply follow the majority or your emotions? What would it cost you to pause and reflect before acting?
?
'Ali did not condemn those who refrained from supporting him; he left them to their choice and later even acknowledged that if their act was a sin, it was small and forgiven. Is there a fellow Muslim you have judged harshly for a difference in opinion or action? Will you give them the benefit of the doubt, trusting that they acted on their best understanding, just as 'Ali did with his Companions?
?
The hadith the Companions relied upon includes the phrase 'the one sitting is better than the one standing' during fitna. Are you willing to sit back from a good deed if you fear it may lead to greater harm or division among Muslims? How do you distinguish between cowardice and wise caution based on the teachings of the Prophet?
