Prophetic Proofs and Claim of Ijma for 'Ali's Caliphate
Maturidi proofs for 'Ali's caliphate using hadith and ijma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three hadith proofs used by Maturidi theologians to support 'Ali's caliphate and the argument from consensus (ijma').

Core Idea
Three hadith and a claim of ijma establish 'Ali's caliphate as truth.

This passage presents the core textual evidence for 'Ali's imamah from the perspective of Maturidi kalam. It cites three prophetic hadith that directly point to 'Ali's legitimacy and the wrongfulness of his opponents. Additionally, it mentions a theological argument that consensus existed on his caliphate. Let's examine each proof and its logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a feel for the sequence of proofs.
2
Identify the first hadith
The Prophet tells 'Ali he will fight three groups: nakithin, mariqin, qasitin. Recognize these as references to historical battles.
These groups correspond to opponents in the Battle of the Camel, Siffin, and the Kharijites.
3
Understand the second hadith
The 30-year caliphate hadith places 'Ali as the fourth caliph completing that period, affirming his rightful succession.
This hadith implies that the caliphate after the Prophet would last 30 years, ending with 'Ali's caliphate.
4
Analyze the third hadith
The hadith about 'Ammar being killed by the baghiya is used as direct proof: 'Ammar was killed under 'Ali's banner, so 'Ali's side is the truth.
5
Consider the claim of ijma'
Some theologians claimed consensus on 'Ali's caliphate, arguing that the shura council limited the imamah to one of six, and the community agreed.
This is an additional argument beyond the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة خلافته أن النبي عليه السلام قال له: "إنك تقتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين" وقال عليه السلام: "الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة" وقد تمت الثلاثون يوم هو، وقال عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" وقد قتل يوم صفين تحت راية علي، ولو لم يكن هو على الحق لما كان من يقاتله باغيًا، والله الموفق. ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ادعوا الإجماع على خلافة علي، وقالوا: إن الإجماع منعقد زمان الشورى على اقتصار الإمامة على أحد الستة الذين كانوا.
Then the proof for the validity of his caliphate (khilafah) is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to him: 'You will fight the breakers of the covenant (al-nakithin), the deviators (al-mariqin), and the oppressors (al-qasitin).' And he (peace be upon him) said: 'The caliphate after me will be thirty years,' and the thirty years were completed on his day. And he (peace be upon him) said to 'Ammar: 'You will be killed by the rebellious faction (al-fiah al-baghiya).' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of 'Ali. If he were not upon the truth, then those who fought him would not be called rebellious. And God is the granter of success. Then, some speculative theologians (mutakallimun) claimed consensus (ijma') on the caliphate of 'Ali, and said: 'Indeed, consensus was established at the time of the council (shura) on restricting the imamah to one of the six who were [selected].'
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage provides direct evidence for the validity of 'Ali's caliphate. It begins by citing three hadith. First, the Prophet told 'Ali that he would fight three groups: the nakithin (those who break allegiance, referring to the Battle of the Camel), the mariqin (those who deviate, referring to the Kharijites), and the qasitin (the oppressors, referring to the army of Muawiya at Siffin). Second, the Prophet said the caliphate after him would last thirty years, and that period ended with 'Ali's caliphate (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali sum to 30 years approximately). Third, the Prophet told 'Ammar ibn Yasir that he would be killed by the rebellious faction (al-fiah al-baghiya), and 'Ammar was killed at the Battle of Siffin fighting under 'Ali's banner. The logic is: if 'Ali were not upon the truth, then those fighting him would not be called 'baghiya' (rebellious/transgressing). The passage concludes with a claim by some mutakallimun that there was consensus (ijma') on 'Ali's caliphate, arguing that the shura (the council to select the caliph after Umar) limited the imamah to one of the six candidates, and the community agreed, thereby establishing 'Ali's legitimacy.

مارقين (mariqin)
قاسطين (qasitin)
فئة باغية (al-fiah al-baghiya)
إجماع (ijma')
شورى (shura)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith about 'Ammar only indicates 'Ali's personal righteousness, not the validity of his caliphate.
Correction: The passage uses it as a direct proof that his side is on the truth, hence his imamah is correct.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT used as a proof for 'Ali's caliphate in this passage?
A) Hadith that 'Ali would fight the nakithin, mariqin, qasitin
B) Hadith that the caliphate after the Prophet is thirty years
C) Hadith that 'Ammar would be killed by the baghiya
D) The consensus of the Ansar

Key Takeaway
Three hadiths and a claim of consensus form the basis for the Maturidi argument that 'Ali's caliphate was divinely sanctioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were living at the time of the conflict between 'Ali and Muawiya, which side would you have chosen based on the prophetic evidence presented? What does that tell you about your willingness to follow truth even when it means opposing the majority or the powerful?
?
The Prophet foretold that 'Ali would fight those who break covenants, deviate, and oppress. In your own life, when you stand for truth, do you recognize that you will face opposition from those who break promises, distort religion, or oppress? How do you prepare your heart to remain steadfast without resorting to their methods?
?
The passage uses ijma as a proof for 'Ali's caliphate. Do you rely on scholarly consensus to determine truth, or do you independently verify evidence? How can you ensure that your loyalty to a particular group or scholar does not blind you to clear proofs from the Qur'an and Sunnah?

The Hadith on Fitna and Ali's Excuse for Those Who Stayed Back
Imam Nasafi relates a hadith about fitna and Ali's sermon excusing those who refrained from supporting him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic hadith about fitna and Ali's own sermon show that those who refrained from supporting him were not sinful and that their inaction did not detract from his imamate.

Core Idea
Staying out of fitna can be virtuous, and Ali himself excused those who stayed back.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's work addresses a sensitive historical point: the status of those who did not support Ali during the civil strife. It presents a prophetic hadith about the virtue of staying out of fitna and then shows Ali's own statement that he did not consider them sinful. This helps us understand the nuanced view of early Islamic history regarding loyalty and dissent.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its grading of positions
Start by reading the prophetic hadith about fitna. Notice how it grades different levels of engagement: sitting, standing, walking, running, and reclining. This sets the context for why some people refrained from fighting.
The hadith is attributed to the Prophet and is used by those who stayed back to justify their action.
2
Understand the people's interpretation and Ali's response
After the hadith, the passage explains that some people interpreted it literally and refrained from supporting Ali. Then it tells us that Ali did not blame them. Read carefully how the author says 'he left them and what they chose for themselves.'
Ali's silence or non-condemnation is used as evidence that their choice was acceptable.
3
Analyze Ali's sermon after the Arbitration
The passage then cites Ali's sermon after the two arbiters (Hakamain). Focus on his words: 'if it be a sin, it is a small forgiven sin; if it be a good deed, it is a great thanked deed.' This is the key proof that Ali did not consider them sinful.
The sermon speaks of Sa'd ibn Malik and Abdullah ibn Amr, who accepted arbitration.
4
Reflect on the implications for imamate and sin
Finally, consider the overall conclusion: that refraining from supporting Ali did not harm his imamate, nor did it make those who refrained guilty of sin. This shows a tolerant approach to disagreement among companions.
This passage addresses a sectarian issue often debated between Sunnis and Shia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويروي هو أيضا عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال: "ستكون بعدي فتنة القاعد فيها خير من القائم والقائم فيها خير من الماشي والماشي فيها خير من الساعي". قال: وأراه قال - والمضطجع فيها خير من القاعد فقعدوا عن نصرته متأولين لهذه الأحاديث فتركهم وما اختاروا هم لأنفسهم، فلم يقدح ذلك في إمامته، ولا كانوا هم بذلك مرتكبين مأثم.
يحققه: أن عليا [؟] الله خطب بعد أمر الحكمين خطبة قال فيها بعد كلام طويل اللهم منزل نزله سعد بن مالك وعبد الله بن عمرو، والله لئن كان ذنبًا إنه لصغير مغفور، وإن كان حسنًا إنه لعظيم مشكور - وهذا تصريح منه أنه لا يرى تأثيمهم في قعودهم عن نصرته.
He also narrates from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'There will be after me a fitna in which the sitting one is better than the standing, and the standing is better than the walking, and the walking is better than the running.' He said: I think he said: 'And the reclining is better than the sitting.' So they refrained from supporting him, interpreting these hadiths, and he left them and what they chose for themselves. That did not detract from his imamate, nor were they thereby committing a sin.
He confirms it: that Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, after the affair of the two arbiters, gave a sermon in which he said, after a long speech: 'O Allah, a place which Sa'd ibn Malik and Abdullah ibn Amr have taken, by Allah, if it be a sin, it is a small, forgiven sin; and if it be a good deed, it is a great, thanked deed.' This is a clear statement from him that he does not consider them sinful for refraining from supporting him.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage provides a hadith and a statement from Ali to show that those who did not support him in the battles were not sinful. It begins by quoting a prophetic hadith about a future fitna where the best position is to sit out, then to stand, then to walk, then to run, and even the reclining is better than sitting. The supporters of Ali who refrained from fighting interpreted this hadith literally, so they stayed back. Ali did not blame them or declare them sinful, as he left them to their choice. The proof is taken from Ali's own sermon after the Arbitration (Hakamain), where he says: 'O Allah, a place which Sa'd ibn Malik and Abdullah ibn Amr have taken...' meaning that their action—accepting arbitration—was either a small sin forgiven or a great good deed thanked. This shows that Ali did not think they were at fault. Thus, their refraining did not damage Ali's imamate, and they were not guilty of sin.

إمامة
مأثم
تحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that those who did not support Ali were necessarily sinful or that their inaction challenged his legitimacy.
Correction: This passage shows that Ali himself excused them and that their refraining did not affect his imamate or make them sinful.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ali's sermon mentioned in this passage?
A) To declare that the arbiters were sinful.
B) To say that he does not consider those who refrained from supporting him as sinful.
C) To praise Sa'd ibn Malik and Abdullah ibn Amr.
D) To criticize the hadith about fitna.

Key Takeaway
This passage demonstrates that refraining from supporting Ali during fitna was not sinful, based on a prophetic hadith and Ali's own excusing statement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between obeying a religious leader and following a hadith that encourages passivity, how do you decide? Have you ever used a religious text to justify staying on the sidelines when action was needed? What would Ali say about your choice?
?
Ali excused those who interpreted the hadith differently, even though they did not support him in battle. Is there someone you have judged for not supporting you in a difficult time? Can you find in your heart the same leniency that Ali showed, or does your pride prevent it?
?
This lesson shows that sincere interpretation can excuse even a failure to support a rightful imam. Do you extend the same charity to others who disagree with you on religious matters, or do you quickly label them as sinful? What would it take for you to admit that you might be wrong and they might be forgiven?

Hadith Proofs for Ali's Caliphate
Nasafi presents three hadiths as proofs for the correctness of Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses three prophetic hadiths to establish the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate.

Core Idea
The Prophet's words identify Ali's opponents as the rebellious group.

Imam Nasafi begins by citing specific hadiths that directly support Ali's position. These prophecies, given by the Prophet himself, indicate that those who fought against Ali were the breakers of pledges, the renegades, and the unjust. This lesson examines these proofs and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation. Notice how each hadith is presented as a proof.
The passage is dense with hadith references.
2
Identify the three hadiths
Locate each hadith: the one about Ali killing three groups, the thirty-year caliphate, and Ammar being killed by the rebellious group.
Each hadith serves a different purpose: labeling opponents, affirming the timeframe, and identifying the rebellious party.
3
Reflect on the argument's logic
Consider how these hadiths collectively prove that Ali was on the truth and his opponents were in the wrong. Why does Nasafi say 'if he were not upon the truth, then those who fight him would not be rebellious'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة خلافته أن النبي عليه السلام قال له: "إنك تقتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين" وقال عليه السلام: "الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة" وقد تمت الثلاثون يوم هو، وقال عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" وقد قتل يوم صفين تحت راية علي، ولو لم يكن هو على الحق لما كان من يقاتله باغيًا، والله الموفق.
ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ادعوا الإجماع على خلافة علي، وقالوا: إن الإجماع منعقد زمان الشورى على اقتصار الإمامة على أحد الستة الذين كانوا من أهل الشورى، ثم بعد ذلك تقرر الرأي على أنها لأحد الرجلين إما علي وإما عثمان، فكان هذا إجماعًا أن الخليفة علي لو ولاه عثمان فإذا خرج عثمان بالقتل من البيت فكان ذلك الإجماع باقيًا على عليه، وقد روينا في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر له أن النبي عليه السلام قال: "وإن وليتم عليًا تجدوه هاديًا مهديًا" فإذا ولي في وقته كان هاديًا مهديًا بشهادة رسول الله عليه السلام، وروى أنه عليه السلام صعد جبل حراء ومعه أبو بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهم أجمعين - فقال عليه السلام: "اسكن حراء فما عليك إلا نبي أو صديق أو شهيد" وفيه دليل أن عمر وعثمان وعليًا قتلوا شهداء، ومن طعن بعد هذا الحديث في أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين فهو الراد على رسول الله عليه السلام قوله، المكذب له في إخباره المنسلخ عن الدين، والأخبار في ذلك أكثر من أن يحيط بها كتابنا هذا، والله سبحانه الموفق.
ثم إن عليا ابتلي بقتال أصحاب الجمل وقتال أهل الشام بصفين وبالتحكيم فنتكلم في كل فصل على وجه يتبين الصواب من الخطأ بمشيئة الله تعالى وعونه.
فأما الكلام في قتال أصحاب الجمل فنقول: إن عليا كان هو المصيب في ذلك لأن إمامته قد كانت ثبتت على ما بينا فكان يجب لغيره الانقياد له والرجوع
Then the proof for the correctness of his caliphate is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to him: 'You will kill the breakers of the pledge, the renegades, and the unjust.' And he said, peace be upon him: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years.' And the thirty years were completed on his day. And he said, peace be upon him, to Ammar: 'The rebellious group will kill you.' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of Ali. And if he were not upon the truth, then those who fight him would not be rebellious. And Allah is the giver of success.
Then some of the mutakallimun claimed consensus on the caliphate of Ali. They said: The consensus at the time of the shura was limited to the imamate being among the six who were the people of the shura. Then after that, opinion settled on it being one of the two men: either Ali or Uthman. So this was a consensus that the caliph was Ali if Uthman appointed him. And when Uthman was killed and removed from the house, that consensus remained on him. And we have narrated in establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'If you make Ali your leader, you will find him a guide and rightly guided.' So when he became leader in his time, he was a guide and rightly guided by the testimony of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. And it is narrated that he, peace be upon him, climbed Mount Hira with Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali—may Allah be pleased with them all—and said, peace be upon him: 'Be still, Hira, for there is upon you only a prophet, a veracious one, or a martyr.' In this is evidence that Umar, Uthman, and Ali were killed as martyrs. And whoever after this hadith attacks any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs is rejecting the statement of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, belittling his report, and stripping himself from the religion. And the reports on this matter are too many to be encompassed by this book of ours. And Allah, exalted, is the giver of success.
Then Ali was tested with fighting the people of the Camel, fighting the people of Syria at Siffin, and the arbitration. We will speak about each chapter in a way that distinguishes right from wrong, by the will of Allah Almighty and His help.
As for the discussion on the fighting of the people of the Camel, we say: Ali was correct in that because his imamate was established as we have clarified, so it was incumbent upon others to submit to him and return.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage provides multiple hadith proofs for the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate and then introduces the Battle of Jamal. It begins by citing the hadith where the Prophet tells Ali that he will kill three groups: the nakithin (those who break the pledge), the mariqin (those who renegade from religion), and the qasitin (the unjust). This indicates that those fighting against Ali are in these categories. Next, the hadith 'The caliphate after me is thirty years' is used; since Ali's reign completes the thirty years (after Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman), his caliphate is thus valid. Then the hadith about Ammar being killed by the rebellious group (al-fi'at al-baghiya) is given; Ammar was killed under Ali's banner at Siffin, proving that those fighting Ali were rebellious and therefore in the wrong. If Ali were not on the truth, his opponents would not be rebellious. The passage then discusses a claim by some mutakallimun that there was consensus on Ali's caliphate: at the time of the shura (the consultative council after Umar's death), the imamate was restricted to the six companions, and later the choice narrowed to Ali or Uthman. Since Uthman was chosen first, and then killed, the consensus that the caliph would be one of those two remained, and with Uthman gone, it applied to Ali. Another hadith states: 'If you make Ali your leader, you will find him a guide and rightly guided,' which establishes that when Ali did become leader, he was indeed guided. The hadith of Mount Hira enumerates the four Caliphs as a prophet (the Prophet himself), a veracious one (Abu Bakr), and martyrs (Umar, Uthman, Ali). This shows their high status and that those who attack them are rejecting the Prophet's words. The passage concludes that the reports on this are numerous. Then the author transitions to the battles: Ali was tested with the Camel, Siffin, and arbitration. He intends to discuss each to show right from wrong. Specifically regarding the Battle of Jamal, he asserts that Ali was correct because his imamate was clearly established, and thus others were obliged to submit to him and return (to obedience).

Key Terms
ناكثين
مارقين
قاسطين
خلافة
ثلاثون سنة
فئة باغية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ali's caliphate was not directly supported by hadith.
Correction: These three hadiths provide direct support, as Nasafi demonstrates.

Checkpoint
Which hadith indicates that those who kill Ammar are the rebellious group?
A) Hadith of thirty years
B) Hadith of Ali killing three groups
C) Hadith about Mount Hira
D) Hadith telling Ammar he will be killed by the rebellious group

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's hadiths directly support Ali's legitimacy and label his opponents as rebellious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Prophet identified Ali's opponents as rebellious, do you find yourself tempted to label those you disagree with today as rebellious too? How can you check your own heart against being the type of person who would fight against the truth while believing you are right?
?
The hadith that the caliphate is thirty years suggests that the prophetic model of governance had a limited time. Does your understanding of political authority today rest on the same prophetic standard, or have you settled for something less? What would it cost you to seek leadership that truly reflects the prophetic model?
?
Ammar was killed under Ali's banner while standing for the truth. If you were placed in a situation where you had to choose between loyalty to a person or group and standing for the truth, what would you do? Have you ever compromised truth for the sake of loyalty? Will you commit now to always side with truth, even if it means losing relationships?

Consensus and Prophetic Witness for the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the Battle of Jamal
Nasafi discusses a consensus argument and two hadiths, then introduces the Battle of Jamal affirming Ali's correctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments from consensus and prophetic testimony that support the caliphates of the first four caliphs, and how these support Ali in the Battle of Jamal.

Core Idea
Prophetic testimony and consensus confirm the legitimacy of the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Imam Nasafi continues by presenting an argument from consensus (ijma) that the caliphate was restricted to the six companions of the shura, and then to Ali or Uthman. He also cites hadiths that testify to the high status of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali. This lesson examines these proofs and then introduces the Battle of Jamal, where Ali's correctness is affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation. Notice the transition from the consensus claim to the hadiths and then to the battle.
The last part on the Battle of Jamal is cut off; we only have the beginning of the argument.
2
Understand the consensus argument
The passage says some mutakallimun claimed consensus that the caliph was one of the six, then narrowed to Ali or Uthman. When Uthman was killed, the consensus remained that Ali was the rightful caliph.
3
Reflect on the hadith of the guide and Mount Hira
The hadith 'If you make Ali your leader, you will find him a guide' shows Ali's leadership is divinely guided. The Mount Hira hadith honors the four caliphs and warns against attacking them.
The Mount Hira hadith is often used to show the high status of the first four caliphs.
4
Consider the Battle of Jamal introduction
Nasafi asserts that Ali was correct in the Battle of Jamal because his imamate was established. The argument is incomplete but sets the stage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة خلافته أن النبي عليه السلام قال له: "إنك تقتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين" وقال عليه السلام: "الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة" وقد تمت الثلاثون يوم هو، وقال عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" وقد قتل يوم صفين تحت راية علي، ولو لم يكن هو على الحق لما كان من يقاتله باغيًا، والله الموفق.
ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ادعوا الإجماع على خلافة علي، وقالوا: إن الإجماع منعقد زمان الشورى على اقتصار الإمامة على أحد الستة الذين كانوا من أهل الشورى، ثم بعد ذلك تقرر الرأي على أنها لأحد الرجلين إما علي وإما عثمان، فكان هذا إجماعًا أن الخليفة علي لو ولاه عثمان فإذا خرج عثمان بالقتل من البيت فكان ذلك الإجماع باقيًا على عليه، وقد روينا في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر له أن النبي عليه السلام قال: "وإن وليتم عليًا تجدوه هاديًا مهديًا" فإذا ولي في وقته كان هاديًا مهديًا بشهادة رسول الله عليه السلام، وروى أنه عليه السلام صعد جبل حراء ومعه أبو بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهم أجمعين - فقال عليه السلام: "اسكن حراء فما عليك إلا نبي أو صديق أو شهيد" وفيه دليل أن عمر وعثمان وعليًا قتلوا شهداء، ومن طعن بعد هذا الحديث في أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين فهو الراد على رسول الله عليه السلام قوله، المكذب له في إخباره المنسلخ عن الدين، والأخبار في ذلك أكثر من أن يحيط بها كتابنا هذا، والله سبحانه الموفق.
ثم إن عليا ابتلي بقتال أصحاب الجمل وقتال أهل الشام بصفين وبالتحكيم فنتكلم في كل فصل على وجه يتبين الصواب من الخطأ بمشيئة الله تعالى وعونه.
فأما الكلام في قتال أصحاب الجمل فنقول: إن عليا كان هو المصيب في ذلك لأن إمامته قد كانت ثبتت على ما بينا فكان يجب لغيره الانقياد له والرجوع
Then the proof for the correctness of his caliphate is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to him: 'You will kill the breakers of the pledge, the renegades, and the unjust.' And he said, peace be upon him: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years.' And the thirty years were completed on his day. And he said, peace be upon him, to Ammar: 'The rebellious group will kill you.' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of Ali. And if he were not upon the truth, then those who fight him would not be rebellious. And Allah is the giver of success.
Then some of the mutakallimun claimed consensus on the caliphate of Ali. They said: The consensus at the time of the shura was limited to the imamate being among the six who were the people of the shura. Then after that, opinion settled on it being one of the two men: either Ali or Uthman. So this was a consensus that the caliph was Ali if Uthman appointed him. And when Uthman was killed and removed from the house, that consensus remained on him. And we have narrated in establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'If you make Ali your leader, you will find him a guide and rightly guided.' So when he became leader in his time, he was a guide and rightly guided by the testimony of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. And it is narrated that he, peace be upon him, climbed Mount Hira with Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali—may Allah be pleased with them all—and said, peace be upon him: 'Be still, Hira, for there is upon you only a prophet, a veracious one, or a martyr.' In this is evidence that Umar, Uthman, and Ali were killed as martyrs. And whoever after this hadith attacks any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs is rejecting the statement of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, belittling his report, and stripping himself from the religion. And the reports on this matter are too many to be encompassed by this book of ours. And Allah, exalted, is the giver of success.
Then Ali was tested with fighting the people of the Camel, fighting the people of Syria at Siffin, and the arbitration. We will speak about each chapter in a way that distinguishes right from wrong, by the will of Allah Almighty and His help.
As for the discussion on the fighting of the people of the Camel, we say: Ali was correct in that because his imamate was established as we have clarified, so it was incumbent upon others to submit to him and return.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage provides multiple hadith proofs for the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate and then introduces the Battle of Jamal. It begins by citing the hadith where the Prophet tells Ali that he will kill three groups: the nakithin (those who break the pledge), the mariqin (those who renegade from religion), and the qasitin (the unjust). This indicates that those fighting against Ali are in these categories. Next, the hadith 'The caliphate after me is thirty years' is used; since Ali's reign completes the thirty years (after Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman), his caliphate is thus valid. Then the hadith about Ammar being killed by the rebellious group (al-fi'at al-baghiya) is given; Ammar was killed under Ali's banner at Siffin, proving that those fighting Ali were rebellious and therefore in the wrong. If Ali were not on the truth, his opponents would not be rebellious. The passage then discusses a claim by some mutakallimun that there was consensus on Ali's caliphate: at the time of the shura (the consultative council after Umar's death), the imamate was restricted to the six companions, and later the choice narrowed to Ali or Uthman. Since Uthman was chosen first, and then killed, the consensus that the caliph would be one of those two remained, and with Uthman gone, it applied to Ali. Another hadith states: 'If you make Ali your leader, you will find him a guide and rightly guided,' which establishes that when Ali did become leader, he was indeed guided. The hadith of Mount Hira enumerates the four Caliphs as a prophet (the Prophet himself), a veracious one (Abu Bakr), and martyrs (Umar, Uthman, Ali). This shows their high status and that those who attack them are rejecting the Prophet's words. The passage concludes that the reports on this are numerous. Then the author transitions to the battles: Ali was tested with the Camel, Siffin, and arbitration. He intends to discuss each to show right from wrong. Specifically regarding the Battle of Jamal, he asserts that Ali was correct because his imamate was clearly established, and thus others were obliged to submit to him and return (to obedience).

شورى
هادي مهدي
صديق
شهيد
أصحاب الجمل
صفين
تحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the consensus argument is weak or that the hadith of Mount Hira applies only to the Prophet.
Correction: Nasafi uses these as evidence for the legitimacy and high status of the caliphs.

Checkpoint
According to the consensus argument, the caliph after Uthman was to be from which group?
A) Any of the four caliphs
B) One of the two: Ali or Uthman
C) Ali only
D) The six companions of the shura

Key Takeaway
Prophetic testimony and consensus affirm the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate, and his position in the Battle of Jamal is correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet testified that Ali would be a 'guide, rightly guided' if made leader. Do you truly believe that the guidance of the Prophet and his rightly guided successors is sufficient for you, or do you rely on other sources? What would it look like for you to submit completely to their guidance today?
?
The hadith of Mount Hira declares that attacking any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs is 'rejecting the statement of the Messenger of Allah, belittling his report, and stripping oneself from the religion.' Examine your heart: Are there any grudges, dismissive thoughts, or casual criticisms you hold towards the Companions or early Muslims? How will you work to remove them?
?
Ali was correct in the Battle of the Camel because his imamate was established and it was incumbent on others to submit. Is there a situation in your life where you know the truth and the rightful authority, but you still hesitate to submit? What is holding you back, and what step will you take to align yourself with the truth?

The Shura Consensus on the Caliphate
Author states the shura consensus and its implication for Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the shura council's decision to support the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate through the concept of consensus.

Core Idea
The shura's consensus is a foundation for the caliphate's legitimacy.

This passage presents a historical argument that the consultative body (shura) narrowed the caliphate to two candidates, Ali and Uthman, which the author treats as a consensus. After Uthman's death, this consensus is claimed to have remained valid for Ali, thereby supporting his caliphate. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shura and consensus
Carefully read the Arabic sentence about the shura and its decision. Notice the key terms 'أهل الشورى' and 'إجماع'. Follow the translation to understand the claim.
The phrase 'فكان هذا إجماعاً' is central.
2
Identify the two candidates
The shura determined the caliphate was for one of two men: Ali or Uthman. Recognize that this sets the stage for the argument.
3
Understand the persistence of consensus
The author argues that even after Uthman's assassination, that earlier consensus remained applicable to Ali. Ask yourself: how does this legitimize Ali's rule?
The logic is based on the idea that consensus, once established, is not easily broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أهل الشورى، ثم بعد ذلك تقرر الرأي على أنها لأحد الرجلين إما علي وإما عثمان، فكان هذا إجماعاً أن الخليفة علي لو ولاه عثمان فإذا خرج عثمان بالقتل من البيت فكان ذلك الإجماع باقيًا على عليه، وقد روينا في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر له أن النبي عليه السلام قال: 'وإن وليتم عليًا تجدوه' هاديًا مهديا (۱) فإذا ولي في وقته كان هاديًا مهديًا بشهادة رسول الله عليه السلام، وروى أنه عليه السلام صعد جبل حراء ومعه أبو بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلي - رضي أجمعين - فقال عليه السلام: 'اسكن' حراء' فما عليك إلا نبي أو صديق أو شهيد (۲) وفيه دليل أن عمر وعثمان وعليًا قتلوا شهداء ) (۳) ومن طعن بعد هذا الحديث في أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين فهو الراد على رسول الله عليه السلام قوله، المكذب له في إخباره المنسلخ عن الدين، والأخبار في (٤) ذلك أكثر من أن يحيط بها كتابنا هذا، والله سبحانه الموفق. الله عنهم
From the people of the shura, then after that the opinion was decided that it (the caliphate) was for one of two men: either Ali or Uthman. So this was a consensus that the caliph Ali if Uthman had appointed him, and when Uthman was killed leaving the house, that consensus remained upon him. And we have narrated in establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr for him that the Prophet عليه السلام said: 'If you appoint Ali as your leader, you will find him' a rightly-guided guide (1). So when he was appointed in his time, he was a rightly-guided guide by the testimony of the Messenger of Allah عليه السلام. And it was narrated that he عليه السلام ascended Mount Hira with Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali - may Allah be pleased with them all - and he عليه السلام said: 'Be still, Hira! For upon you is none but a prophet, a truthful one, or a martyr' (2). And in this is evidence that Umar, Uthman, and Ali were killed as martyrs (3). And whoever criticizes after this hadith any one of the rightly-guided caliphs is rejecting the statement of the Messenger of Allah عليه السلام, belying him in his report, and divesting himself of the religion. And the reports on this (4) are too many for this book to encompass. And Allah, glorified be He, is the grantor of success. May Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a combination of historical argument and hadith proof. The author first states that the shura (consultative body) narrowed the caliphate to Ali or Uthman, implying that Ali's eventual caliphate was based on this consensus. Then he cites a hadith showing that the Prophet declared that if Ali is appointed, he will be a righteous guide. Next, he presents the famous hadith of Mount Hira, where the Prophet mentions that on the mountain are only a prophet, a truthful one (siddiq), or a martyr (shahid). The Prophet was present, as were Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali. The author concludes that this hadith proves Umar, Uthman, and Ali were martyred (Umar was killed by a Persian slave, Uthman was assassinated, and Ali was killed by a Kharijite). He then declares that anyone who criticizes any of the rightly-guided caliphs after this hadith is in effect rejecting the Prophet's words and leaving the religion. He notes that there are many such narrations, but the book cannot contain them all. The passage ends with a prayer for the caliphs.

Key Terms
أهل الشورى
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ali's caliphate had no prior consensus or was purely based on later events.
Correction: The author explicitly argues that the shura's earlier consensus included Ali as a candidate, and that consensus remained effective.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the shura decide about the caliphate?
A) It was for Ali alone.
B) It was for Uthman alone.
C) It was for one of two men: Ali or Uthman.
D) It was for Abu Bakr.

Key Takeaway
The shura's consensus, which included Ali as a candidate, is used to argue for the legitimacy of his caliphate after Uthman's death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the conflicts in the early Muslim community, do you tend to side with one figure over another based on emotion rather than the actual evidence of consensus (like the one mentioned here)? What would it take for you to accept a decision you initially disagreed with if it was based on collective agreement?
?
The author argues that a consensus from one time period (the shura) remained valid even after a major event (Uthman's death). In your own life, are there commitments or promises you made that you have allowed to lapse because circumstances changed, even though the original agreement still applies? How would your relationships change if you held to that original consensus?
?
If you were in the position of Ali, knowing that the consensus of the shura still designated you as caliph, would you have pursued the office or stepped aside to avoid further discord? What does your answer reveal about your trust in collective decision-making versus personal ambition?

The Hadith of Mount Hira and the Caliphs' Status
Author cites hadiths to prove the caliphs' virtue and warns against criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hadith of Mount Hira is used to establish the high status of the four caliphs, especially their martyrdom, and the severe warning against criticizing them.

Core Idea
The Prophet's testimony on Mount Hira affirms the caliphs as truthful and martyrs.

The author cites a well-known hadith where the Prophet, standing on Mount Hira with Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali, declares that only a prophet, a truthful one, or a martyr are present. This is used to prove that Abu Bakr is the truthful one (siddiq) and that Umar, Uthman, and Ali were martyred. The passage then warns that criticizing any of the caliphs after this is akin to rejecting the Prophet's words.

Guided Path
1
Read the two hadith passages
First, read the hadith about Ali being a 'rightly-guided guide', then the Mount Hira hadith. Note how they are connected.
The first hadith reinforces Ali's righteousness; the second establishes the status of all four.
2
Identify the categories in the Mount Hira hadith
The Prophet mentions three categories: prophet, truthful one (siddiq), and martyr (shahid). Determine who fits each according to the author.
Abu Bakr is the siddiq; Umar, Uthman, and Ali are martyrs.
3
Understand the doctrinal conclusion
The author explicitly states this hadith proves that Umar, Uthman, and Ali were killed as martyrs. Recognize the significance.
This is a direct claim about their deaths.
4
Reflect on the warning
Read the severe statement about criticizing any caliph after this hadith. Understand why such criticism is equated with rejecting the Prophet.
The author uses strong language: 'الراد على رسول الله' and 'المنسلخ عن الدين'.

?

?

Does your heart truly love and respect Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali as the Prophet honored them? What practical step can you take today to strengthen that love in your heart and reject any doubt?

Ali's Correctness in the Battles of the Camel and Siffin
Author argues Ali was correct in fighting at Jamal and Siffin, citing his imamate and a prophetic hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that Ali was correct in fighting at the Battles of the Camel and Siffin, based on his established imamate and a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Ali fought as the rightful imam, just as the Prophet fought on revelation — both were correct.

The author begins by defending Ali's actions in two major battles that occurred during his caliphate. He argues that since Ali's imamate was valid, he had both the right and duty to fight those who opposed him. This lesson examines that argument and a key hadith that reinforces it.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for Ali's correctness
Read the first part of the passage where the author explains why Ali was correct in fighting the people of the Camel, based on his imamate.
Notice the logical flow: imamate established → duty to obey → imam's duty to call → if not, fight.
2
Examine the prophetic hadith
Read the hadith where the Prophet tells Ali that he fights on ta'wil just as the Prophet fought on tanzil. Understand how this supports Ali's position.
This hadith is a key proof that Ali was on the truth.
3
Connect to Siffin
The author states the same reasoning applies to the Battle of Siffin. Note how the hadith applies to both battles.

ثم إن عليا الله ابتلى بقتال أصحاب الجمل (۹) وقتال أهل الشام بصفين (٦) وبالتحكيم فنتكلم في كل فصل على وجه يتبين الصواب من الخطأ بمشيئة الله تعالى وعونه. عليه وموقعة الجمل وصفين فأما الكلام في قتال أصحاب الجمل فنقول : إن عليا لله الله كان هو المصيب في ذلك لأن إمامته قد كانت ثبتت على ما بينا فكان يجب لغيره الانقياد له والرجوع إلى طاعته، ومن أبى إلا الإصرار على المخالفة كان على الإمام أن يدعوه إلى الطاعة ويبين (۱) له خطأ ما هو عليه من الرأي، وما يتولد من ذلك من الضرر بتفريق كلمة الحق وما فيه من شق عصا المسلمين، فإن لم يرجع عن ذلك كان له أن يقاتله حتى يفيء إلى أمر الله، فهو قاتلهم مصيبا في قتالهم مقيمًا ما عليه من حق الله تعالى إذ لم يكن لأحد منازعته في ذلك لثبوت إمامته لما مر من الدلائل، وكذا هذا في قتال أهل صفين. يحققه المروى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال له: 'إنك تقاتل على التأويل كما (۲) تقاتل على التنزيل (۳) ثم كان قتاله عليه السلام على التنزيل وهو لمحق فيه، فكذا عليه في قتاله على التأويل يكون المحق في قتاله، وما(٤) يزعمون أن طلحة والزبير كانا مكرهين على البيعة فاسد لثبوت النقل أن بيعتهما كانت عن طوع على أن خلافته قبل بيعتها كانت ثابتة وما يروى أن طلحة أول من صفعت (٥) يده على يد علي الله فالمراد منه أول يد صفعت يده من أيدي أهل المسجد أو ظن هذا الرأي الراوي أنها أول يد له، لأنه لم يكن حضر البيعة ممن سبق ذكره عند العشاء، وبيعة طلحة صل الله كانت عند الغداة من غد يوم البيعة، وما أنهم قالوا: بايعناك على أن تقتل قتلة عثمان شيء فاسد، فإن قتلة عثمان كانوا روي بغاة، إذ الباغي من له منعة وتأويل، فيقاتل على تأويله الفاسد، ومنعة أولئك ظاهرة، وكانوا في قتله متأولين، إذ كانوا يستحلون ذلك بما نقموا منه من الأمور، والحكم
Then indeed Ali (may Allah?) was tested with fighting the people of the Camel (9) and fighting the people of Syria at Siffin (6) and with arbitration. So we will speak in each chapter in a manner that reveals right from wrong by the will and help of Allah, exalted. Upon him and the battle of the Camel and Siffin. As for the speech regarding fighting the people of the Camel, we say: Indeed Ali (??) was correct in that because his imamate had been established as we have explained, so it was obligatory for others to submit to him and return to his obedience. And whoever refuses except insisting on opposition, it is upon the imam to call him to obedience and clarify (1) to him the error of his opinion and the harm resulting from it in dividing the word of truth and what it involves of splitting the unity of the Muslims. Then if he does not desist from that, he (the imam) may fight him until he returns to the command of Allah. So he (Ali) fought them, correct in his fighting them, fulfilling what was incumbent upon him of the right of Allah, since no one had the right to dispute that due to the confirmation of his imamate by the proofs that have passed. And likewise this is in the fighting of the people of Siffin. This is confirmed by what is narrated from the Prophet عليه السلام that he said to him: 'You fight on ta'wil (interpretation) just as (2) you fight on tanzil (revelation)' (3). Then his fighting عليه السلام was on tanzil and he was the party of truth in it. So likewise Ali in his fighting on ta'wil is the party of truth in his fighting. And what they claim (4) that Talha and al-Zubayr were coerced into the pledge of allegiance is false, because the transmission is established that their pledge was voluntary, and that his caliphate before their pledge was established. And what is narrated that Talha was the first whose hand was struck (5) upon the hand of Ali (??) — the intended meaning is the first hand struck upon his hand from the hands of the people of the mosque, or this narrator thought it was his first hand because he had not attended the pledge of those mentioned previously at the evening meal. And the pledge of Talha (??) was at the morning of the day following the day of the pledge. And what they say: 'We pledged allegiance to you on condition that you kill the killers of Uthman' is a corrupt thing. For the killers of Uthman were (reported?) transgressors (bughat). Indeed, the transgressor (baghi) is one who has power and an interpretation (ta'wil). So he fights based on his corrupt interpretation. And the power of those was apparent, and they in killing him were interpreters (muta'awwilin), since they deemed it permissible by what they criticized of his affairs. And the judgment...
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument defending Ali's actions in the Battles of the Camel and Siffin, followed by rebuttals of two objections. The author begins by stating that Ali was tested with the Battles of the Camel and Siffin and the arbitration; he will address each to show right from wrong. First, regarding the Battle of the Camel, he argues that Ali was correct because his imamate was established. Therefore, others were obliged to submit to him. If someone persisted in opposition, the imam should invite them to obedience and explain the error and harm of dividing the community. If they still refuse, the imam may fight them until they return to Allah's command. Ali did this, fulfilling his duty, and no one could dispute his action because his imamate was proven. The same applies to Siffin. The author then reinforces this with a hadith: the Prophet told Ali that he fights on ta'wil (interpretation) just as the Prophet fought on tanzil (revelation). Since the Prophet was the party of truth in his fight, Ali is likewise the party of truth. Next, the author refutes two objections. First, the claim that Talha and al-Zubayr were coerced into pledging allegiance to Ali is false because reliable reports show they pledged voluntarily. The narration that Talha was the first to shake Ali's hand is interpreted as meaning the first among the people of the mosque, not overall, because Talha did not attend the earlier evening pledge but pledged the next morning. Second, the condition that Ali must kill Uthman's killers is invalid because the killers were bughat (transgressors with a power base and an interpretation). A baghi is defined as one who has power (man'a) and an interpretation (ta'wil); he fights based on his corrupt interpretation. The killers had power and were muta'awwilin (interpreters) who deemed Uthman's killing lawful due to grievances. The passage ends with 'wal-hukm' (the judgment), left incomplete.

Key Terms
أصحاب الجمل
صفين
إمامة
تأويل
تنزيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ali was wrong to fight other Companions because they were also righteous.
Correction: The author argues that since Ali was the rightful imam, he had the duty to fight those who opposed him in order to maintain unity. The hadith confirms he was on the truth.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Ali's correctness in the Battle of the Camel?
A) He was stronger militarily
B) His imamate was established
C) He had a personal grudge
D) The Prophet directly commanded him

Key Takeaway
Ali's correctness in the Battles of the Camel and Siffin is grounded in his established imamate and confirmed by a prophetic analogy to the Prophet's own combat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you resisted submitting to religious authority because it was uncomfortable or went against your opinion? What does this lesson's emphasis on obedience to the Imam teach you about your own attitude?
?
If you were present in the Battle of the Camel, would you have sided with Ali despite your love for Aisha? Why or why not? This lesson tells you that Ali was correct—does your heart fully accept that, or do you still have reservations?
?
Think of a disagreement with someone in your community about a religious matter. Are you more concerned with being right or with preserving unity? The lesson shows the imam explains the harm of division before fighting—how can you apply that patience and clarity to your own conflicts?

Refuting Objections about Ali's Battles
Author refutes claims of coercion and a condition to kill Uthman's killers, defining bughat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's rebuttals of two key objections against Ali in the Battles of the Camel and Siffin: the claim of coerced pledges and the condition to kill Uthman's killers.

Core Idea
Claims of coercion and a conditional pledge are baseless; the killers of Uthman were rebels with their own interpretation.

After establishing Ali's general correctness in the battles, the author addresses two specific objections: that Talha and Zubayr were forced to pledge allegiance to Ali, and that Ali was obligated to kill Uthman's killers. This lesson unpacks his refutations, including the definition of a transgressor (baghi).

Guided Path
1
Read the first rebuttal about coercion
Read the part where the author rejects the claim that Talha and al-Zubayr were coerced into pledging allegiance. Note the evidence he provides.
He uses transmitted reports that their pledge was voluntary.
2
Understand the interpretation of the handshake narration
The author explains a narration that Talha was the first to shake hands. He clarifies it means among the people of the mosque, not overall.
3
Read the second rebuttal about Uthman's killers
Read the author's argument that the condition to kill Uthman's killers is invalid because the killers were bughat (transgressors with power and interpretation).
Learn the definition of baghi: one who has power (man'a) and an interpretation (ta'wil).

The Imam's Duty Towards Rebels
Nasafi states the imam's duty towards rebels and justifies fighting them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preliminary steps a legitimate imam must take before fighting rebels, and the justification for such action.

Core Idea
The imam must first call rebels to obedience; only then may he fight them.

Nasafi addresses a critical issue in Islamic political theology: how should a legitimate imam deal with rebels (bughat)? He outlines a clear procedure: first call them to obedience, explain their error, and if they persist, then fight them to restore unity. This passage grounds the imam's authority in prior proofs and cites the Battle of Siffin as an example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Pay attention to the sequence of actions described.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if he does not return, then he may fight him.'
2
Identify the imam's first duty
What must the imam do before any fighting? Locate the phrase 'call him to obedience' and note the accompanying explanation.
3
Understand the condition for fighting
The imam may fight only if the rebel persists. Find the phrase that sets this condition.
The fighting is a last resort, not a first response.
4
Examine the justification
Why is the imam correct in fighting? The text says because his imamate is established by proofs. Reflect on this reasoning.
This links back to earlier proofs of legitimacy.
5
Consider the example of Siffin
The passage ends by comparing this ruling to the Battle of Siffin. Think about why this example is chosen.
Siffin is a well-known historical case of fighting rebels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى طاعته، ومن أبى إلا الإصرار على المخالفة كان على الإمام أن يدعوه إلى الطاعة ويبين له خطأ ما هو عليه من الرأي، وما يتولد من ذلك من الضرر بتفريق كلمة الحق وما فيه من شق عصا المسلمين، فإن لم يرجع عن ذلك كان له أن يقاتله حتى يفيء إلى أمر الله، فهو قاتلهم مصيبا في قتالهم مقيمًا ما عليه من حق الله تعالى إذ لم يكن لأحد منازعته في ذلك لثبوت إمامته لما مر من الدلائل، وكذا هذا في قتال أهل صفين.
To his obedience, and whoever refuses except persistence in opposition, then it is upon the imam to call him to obedience and explain to him the error of his opinion, and what results from that of harm in dividing the word of truth and what it entails of splitting the stick of the Muslims. If he does not return from that, he may fight him until he returns to the command of God. So he fights them, being correct in his fighting them, fulfilling what is upon him of the right of God Almighty, since no one can dispute with him in that due to the establishment of his imamate by the proofs that have passed. And likewise this in the fighting of the people of Siffin.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage defines the duty of a legitimate imam towards rebels (al-bughat). It states that the imam must first call them to obedience and clarify the error of their ways and the harm they cause to Muslim unity. If they persist, he is permitted to fight them until they return to obedience. The imam is correct in this fighting because his imamate is established by prior proofs. The Battle of Siffin is given as an example of such legitimate fighting.

Key Terms
باغي
إمام
طاعة
قتال
صفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fighting rebels is always forbidden in Islam.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is the imam's duty to fight rebels after calling them to obedience and if they persist, and the imam is correct in doing so.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must the imam do before fighting rebels?
A) Immediately fight them.
B) Call them to obedience and explain their error.
C) Ignore them.
D) Seek permission from other scholars.

Key Takeaway
The imam must first call rebels to obedience and explain their error; only then may he fight them to restore unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim persisting in what you believe is error, do you first lovingly call them to truth and explain the harm, or do you immediately condemn and distance yourself? Are you willing to follow the imam's patient method even if they reject your call?
?
Have you ever been so convinced of your own rightness that you fought an argument without first seeking to explain and unify? What would it cost you to prioritize unity over being 'right' in your next disagreement?
?
If you were in a position of authority, would you have the self-restraint to warn and explain before using force—or would your ego demand immediate action? How can you cultivate the imam's sense of responsibility before God in your own leadership?

The Voluntariness of Talha and Zubayr's Bay'a
Nasafi refutes a sectarian accusation about Talha and Zubayr's bay'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the accusation that Talha and Zubayr were forced to give bay'a to Ali, using transmitted evidence and a careful interpretation of a key narration.

Core Idea
Bay'a is valid only if voluntary; forced allegiance carries no weight.

This passage addresses a historical accusation against the companions. Nasafi draws on established transmissions to argue that Talha and Zubayr's bay'a to Ali was voluntary, not coerced. He also clarifies a narration about Talha being the first to give bay'a, showing it refers to a specific context. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: bay'a, ikrah (force), and taw' (voluntary).
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage first dismisses the claim of force, then provides a positive argument about the caliphate's validity. Find these two points.
The first argument relies on transmitted reports; the second asserts that Ali's caliphate was already established.
3
Analyze the clarification about Talha
Examine the narration about Talha's hand. Understand how Nasafi interprets it to avoid the implication that Talha was the very first to pledge.
Nasafi offers two possible interpretations: (1) first among those present at that time, or (2) the narrator's mistake.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why this refutation matters for Sunni theology: it upholds the integrity of the companions and the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن طلحة والزبير كانا مكرهين على البيعة فاسد لثبوت النقل أن بيعتهما كانت عن طوع على أن خلافته قبل بيعتها كانت ثابتة وما يروى أن طلحة أول من صفعت يده على يد علي رضي الله عنه فالمراد منه أول يد صفعت يده من أيدي أهل المسجد أو ظن هذا الرأي الراوي أنها أول يد له، لأنه لم يكن حضر البيعة ممن سبق ذكره عند العشاء، وبيعة طلحة رضي الله عنه كانت عند الغداة من غد يوم البيعة.
And what they claim that Talha and al-Zubayr were forced to give the pledge (bay'a) is false due to the established transmission that their bay'a was voluntary, and that his (Ali's) caliphate was established before their bay'a. And what is narrated that Talha's hand was the first to be struck upon the hand of Ali, may God be pleased with him, the meaning is the first hand from among the people of the mosque that was struck, or the narrator thought that it was his first hand because he had not attended the bay'a among those mentioned earlier at the evening meal. And Talha's bay'a, may God be pleased with him, was in the morning of the day after the bay'a.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage refutes the accusation that Talha and Zubayr were forced to give bay'a to Ali. It presents two arguments: first, there is reliable transmission that their bay'a was voluntary, and moreover Ali's caliphate was already valid before their bay'a. Second, the narration stating Talha was the first to give bay'a is interpreted to mean the first among those present at that time, not the absolute first, since Talha actually gave bay'a the following morning.

Key Terms
بيعة
إكراه
طوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the narration 'Talha was the first to give bay'a' means he was absolutely the first to pledge allegiance to Ali.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means the first among the people of the mosque at that specific gathering, or it was a mistake by the narrator because Talha had not been present earlier.

Checkpoint
What does the passage argue about Talha's bay'a to Ali?
A) He was forced.
B) He was the first to give bay'a absolutely.
C) His bay'a was voluntary and occurred the next morning.
D) He did not give bay'a.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the claim of forced bay'a by affirming its voluntariness and clarifying a problematic narration through contextual interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim about someone you already distrust, do you accept it uncritically? How does Nasafi’s reliance on established transmission challenge you to verify even negative claims about others?
?
The passage emphasizes that Ali’s caliphate was valid regardless of Talha and Zubayr’s pledge. Is there a relationship or commitment in your life whose validity you’ve made dependent on others’ approval? How can you shift your focus to what is already true?
?
Talha’s bay'a was voluntary and came from his own will. Have you ever made a promise or pledge under social pressure rather than genuine conviction? How does this lesson about the necessity of voluntariness in bay'a call you to examine your own commitments?

Refuting the Conditional Bay'a: The Ruling on Rebels
Nasafi refutes the conditional bay'a and explains the ruling on rebels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the conditional bay'a of Talha and Zubayr and the ruling on rebels who submit to the imam.

Core Idea
Rebels with power and interpretation are not liable upon submission; thus the condition was invalid.

This lesson examines a key historical and legal argument in Sunni political theology: the claim that Talha and Zubayr stipulated that Ali must kill Uthman's murderers as a condition of their bay'a. Nasafi systematically deconstructs this claim by classifying the killers as rebels (bughat) and explaining the legal rulings that apply. This passage is a masterclass in applying usul al-fiqh and siyasa shar'iyya to historical events.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical claim
Read the opening sentence to grasp the claim being refuted: that Talha and Zubayr made their bay'a conditional on killing Uthman's murderers.
This sets the stage for Nasafi's refutation.
2
Analyze the definition of bughat
Identify the two criteria Nasafi uses to define rebels: having power (man'a) and an interpretation (ta'wil). Notice how he applies this to Uthman's killers.
This is the key legal classification.
3
Grasp the ruling upon submission
Read the ruling that rebels who submit are not held liable for past damages. Then consider the alternative view and the conditions under which accountability may apply.
This leads to the conclusion that the demand on Ali was invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أنهم قالوا: بايعناك على أن تقتل قتلة عثمان شيء فاسد، فإن قتلة عثمان كانوا رؤي بغاة، إذ الباغي من له منعة وتأويل، فيقاتل على تأويله الفاسد، ومنعة أولئك ظاهرة، وكانوا في قتله متأولين، إذ كانوا يستحلون ذلك بما نقموا منه من الأمور، والحكم في الباغي أنه إذا انقاد لإمام أهل العدل لا يؤخذ بما سبق منه من إتلاف أموال أهل العدل وسفك دمائهم وجرح أبدانهم، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك أني يستقيم لهم هذا الشرط عليه، وعند بعض الفقهاء أنه كان يؤاخذ بذلك إلا أن الاشتراط على الإمام أن يفعل بأحد الاجتهادين لا محالة فاسد وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن لأحد أن يطلب ذلك من علي رضي الله عنه ولا كان واجبًا عليهم قتلهم، ولا دفعهم إلى طالب، على أن عند من يرى الباغي مؤاخذا بذلك إنما يوجب على الإمام استيفاء ذلك منهم عند انكسار شوكتهم، وتفرق منعتهم، ووقوع الأمن له عن إثارة الفتنة، وإيقاع الناس في الهرج، وإعضال الداء، وتفاقم الأمر على المسلمين، ولم يكن شيء من هذه المعاني حاصلاً بل كانت الشوكة لهم باقية والقوة ظاهرية بادية والمنعة على حالتها قائمة وعزائم القوم على الخروج على من طالبهم بدمه دائمة، وعند تحقق هذه الأسباب تقتضي السياسة الفاضلة والتدبير الصائب والنظر التام لعامة أهل الإسلام الإعراض عما فعلوا، والإعراض عن مطالبتهم بما استوجبوا، فكيف وليست عليهم تبعة ولا للإمام قِبَلَهم - على أصح القولين وأقوى المذهبين – مطالبة؟!
And as for their saying: 'We gave you bay'a on condition that you kill the murderers of Uthman' - it is false. For the murderers of Uthman were considered rebels (bughat), since the rebel is one who has power and an interpretation (ta'wil), so he fights based on his false interpretation. The power of those was manifest, and they were acting on interpretation in killing him, since they considered it permissible due to what they criticized of his actions. And the ruling concerning the rebel is that when he submits to the imam of justice, he is not taken to account for what preceded from him of destroying the property of the people of justice, shedding their blood, and wounding their bodies. If the matter is so, how can this condition upon him be valid? And according to some jurists, he would be held accountable for that, but the condition on the imam to act according to one of the two ijtihads is necessarily invalid. If so, then no one could demand that from Ali, nor was it obligatory upon them to kill them or hand them over to the claimant. Moreover, according to those who hold the rebel accountable, that only obligates the imam to exact that from them when their power is broken, their protection scattered, and safety from inciting fitna is assured, lest people fall into chaos and the disease become complicated and the matter become difficult for the Muslims. None of these conditions were present; rather, their power remained, their strength was apparent, their protection was intact, and the resolve of the people was constant to revolt against anyone who demanded blood from them. When these reasons are realized, the proper policy, sound management, and complete consideration for all Muslims require turning away from what they did and refraining from demanding what they deserved. How so, when they have no liability and the imam has no claim against them—according to the soundest of the two opinions and the strongest of the two schools—is there a claim?
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Talha and Zubayr's bay'a was conditional on Ali killing Uthman's murderers. It classifies Uthman's killers as rebels (bughat) because they had power and a (false) interpretation (ta'wil). The ruling on rebels is that when they submit to the imam, they are not liable for past damages (according to the majority view). Even according to those who hold rebels accountable, the imam should only pursue them when it is safe and their power is broken. None of those conditions were present at the time of Ali, so the demand was invalid. The passage concludes that the soundest opinion is that Ali had no claim against them.

Key Terms
بغاة
منعة
تأويل
سياسة
فتنة
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rebels are always held accountable for their crimes immediately upon submission.
Correction: The passage states that the majority view is that rebels are not liable for past damages when they submit. Even the minority view only allows accountability when it is safe and the rebels' power is broken.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view in the passage, what is the ruling on a rebel who submits to the imam?
A) He is killed.
B) He is not held liable for past damages.
C) He must pay compensation.
D) He is imprisoned.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on rebels depends on their status upon submission and the prevailing conditions; sound policy may require overlooking past actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The rebels had a false interpretation but sincerely believed it was right. When have you justified a wrong action by convincing yourself it was permissible? How does this lesson expose the danger of relying on your own reasoning without submitting to authority? Will you repent and submit?
?
Ali did not punish the killers because the community would have been harmed. Are there people in your life you are eager to 'punish' or hold accountable, even though doing so might cause greater division or fitna? Can you trust Allah's timing and prioritize unity over your desire for justice?
?
The soundest opinion says the rebels had no liability at all. Does that feel unfair to you? Search your heart: do you truly believe in mercy for those who have wronged others, even if they never face consequences in this world? How can you extend that same mercy to someone who has harmed you?

Validity of Ali's Caliphate and Removal of Blame
Nasafi concludes the validity of Ali's caliphate and his blamelessness.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi concludes that Ali's caliphate is valid and that he is not blameworthy for not pursuing Uthman's killers.

Core Idea
Ali's caliphate is valid; he is not to blame for restraint.

After a detailed discussion on the events surrounding the caliphate, Imam Nasafi delivers a concise conclusion. He affirms the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate and clears him of any blame for not actively seeking retribution against Uthman's murderers. This short but significant statement summarizes a key point in Sunni theological and historical perspective.

The lesson depends on prior knowledge of the historical disputes.
4

This guards against a specific misunderstanding.

Talha and Zubayr: Mistake, Not Sin
Nasafi states that Talha and Zubayr erred but acted according to ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars distinguish between a mistaken ijtihad and a sinful action, using the example of Talha and Zubayr.

Core Idea
Mistakes from qualified mujtahids are errors, not sins.

The early Muslim community faced conflicts among the Companions, raising questions about their moral status. Sunni theologians like Nasafi provide a nuanced principle: when a Companion makes a mistake in ijtihad, it remains an error but is excused. Let's examine the exact words of Imam al-Nasafi on Talha and Zubayr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the text is incomplete at the end, but the key point is clear.
The passage cuts off after 'على', but the sense is that apparent evidences pointed to retaliation.
2
Identify key terms
Find the words khatā' (mistake), ijtihad (independent reasoning), and ahl al-ijtihad (people of ijtihad). These are central to the argument.
Understanding these terms helps grasp the theological nuance.
3
Understand the distinction
Nasafi says their action was a mistake, but they acted based on ijtihad. Contrast this with a sinful act: a mistake by a mujtahid is not a sin.
This is a key principle in Sunni theology regarding the Companions.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters: it protects the status of the Companions while acknowledging human error, maintaining unity in the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما أمر طلحة والزبير فقد كان خطأ عندنا غير أنهما فعلا ما فعلا عن اجتهاد، وهما كانا من أهل الاجتهاد إذ ظاهر الدلائل يوجب القصاص على [؟]
As for the matter of Talha and al-Zubayr, it was a mistake in our view, but they did what they did based on ijtihad, and they were among the people of ijtihad, since the apparent evidences required retaliation against [؟]
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage gives a final verdict on Talha and Zubayr's opposition to Ali. Nasafi states that their action was a mistake (khatā') according to the Ahl al-Sunna, but they are excused because they acted based on their own ijtihad (independent reasoning) as qualified mujtahids. The passage cuts off but likely continued with the justification that the apparent evidences suggested that the murderers of Uthman should be retaliated against, which led them to their stance.

Key Terms
خطأ
اجتهاد
أهل الاجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Companions who opposed Ali are sinful and blameworthy.
Correction: Sunni theology holds that while their action was a mistake, they are excused because they were qualified mujtahids acting on ijtihad.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi characterize the actions of Talha and Zubayr?
A) Unforgivable sin.
B) A mistake but based on ijtihad, so excused.
C) Completely correct.
D) They were forced to oppose Ali.

Key Takeaway
Talha and Zubayr's opposition to Ali was a mistake, but as qualified mujtahids, they are not sinful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you were in a situation where you made a sincere but mistaken judgment about a religious matter, how would you want others to treat you? Are you willing to extend that same courtesy to those you disagree with, especially when they are knowledgeable?
?
This lesson teaches that a qualified person's mistake is not a sin. Do you qualify yourself to judge others' actions? Will you pause before labeling someone a sinner, remembering that you may not know the level of their ijtihad?
