Ruling on the Rebel and Invalid Condition on the Imam
Imam al-Nasafi discusses the ruling on a rebel who submits to the Imam and defends Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the default ruling on a rebel who submits to the just Imam, why the condition on the Imam to follow one of two ijtihads is invalid, and how this defends Ali's caliphate.

Core Idea
A rebel who submits is not liable; the condition to follow one ijtihad is invalid; Ali's caliphate is correct.

Imam al-Nasafi addresses a complex historical and theological issue involving rebels (baghi), the just Imam, and the contested caliphate of Ali (radiyallahu anhu). In this passage, he argues that once a rebel submits, past offenses are not held against them, and critically, any condition requiring the Imam to act on only one of two valid ijtihads is necessarily invalid. This reasoning clears Ali from blame for not pursuing Uthman's killers and affirms the correctness of his caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation to understand the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three key rulings
Look for the default ruling on a submissive rebel, the invalidity of the condition on the Imam, and the conclusion about Ali's caliphate.
Notice how the author uses 'فاسد' (invalid) for the condition.
3
Understand the historical context
Reflect on how this argument defends Ali from criticism regarding the killers of Uthman.
This connects the legal discussion to a major historical controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الباغي أنه إذا انقاد لإمام أهل العدل لا يؤخذ بما سبق منه من إتلاف أموال أهل العدل وسفك دمائهم وجرح أبدانهم، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك أني يستقيم لهم هذا الشرط عليه، وعند بعض الفقهاء أنه كان يؤاخذ بذلك إلا أن الاشتراط على الإمام أن يفعل بأحد الاجتهادين لا محالة فاسد وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن لأحد أن يطلب ذلك من علي [؟] ولا كان واجبًا عليهم قتلهم، ولا دفعهم إلى طالب، على أن عند من يرى الباغي مؤاخذا بذلك إنما يوجب على الإمام استيفاء ذلك منهم عند انكسار شوكتهم، وتفرق منعتهم، ووقوع الأمن له عن إثارة الفتنة، وإيقاع الناس في الهرج، وإعضال الداء، وتفاقم الأمر على المسلمين، ولم يكن شيء من هذه المعاني حاصلاً بل كانت الشوكة لهم باقية والقوة ظاهرية بادية والمنعة على حالتها قائمة وعزائم القوم على الخروج على من طالبهم بدمه دائمة، وعند تحقق هذه الأسباب تقتضي السياسة الفاضلة والتدبير الصائب والنظر التام لعامة أهل الإسلام الإعراض عما فعلوا، والإعراض عن مطالبتهم بما استوجبوا، فكيف وليست عليهم تبعة ولا للإمام قبلهم - على أصح القولين وأقوى المذهبين – مطالبة؟!. وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة ظهر صحة خلافة علي له، واندفاع اللائمة عنه في تركه التعرض لقتلة عثمان
Regarding the rebel (al-baghi): when he submits to the Imam of the people of justice, he is not taken to task for what preceded from him of destruction of the wealth of the people of justice, shedding of their blood, and wounding of their bodies. If the matter is so, how can this condition (al-shart) be valid for them? According to some jurists, he would be held accountable for that, except that the condition (al-ishrat) upon the Imam that he act according to one of the two ijtihads is necessarily invalid. If that is so, no one could demand that from Ali [?], nor was it obligatory upon them to kill them, nor to hand them over to a claimant. However, according to those who hold the rebel accountable for that, it only obligates the Imam to exact that from them when their power is broken, their supporters are dispersed, security from inciting fitna is achieved, and to avoid plunging people into chaos, exacerbating the disease, and aggravation of the matter upon the Muslims. None of these meanings were obtained; rather, their power remained, their strength was apparent and manifest, their support remained as it was, and the determination of the people to rebel against whoever demanded blood from them was continuous. When these reasons are realized, virtuous policy, sound management, and complete consideration for the generality of the people of Islam necessitate turning away from what they did and refraining from demanding what they deserved. How (can it be otherwise) when they bear no liability, nor does the Imam have any claim against them – according to the soundest of the two sayings and the strongest of the two schools? By understanding this summary, the correctness of the caliphate of Ali appears, and blame is repelled from him for refraining from pursuing the killers of Uthman.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument about the legal status of rebels (baghi) who submit to the just Imam. It begins by stating the default ruling: if the rebel submits, he is not held accountable for past offenses. Then it addresses a condition (al-ishrat) some might impose on the Imam to follow one of two ijtihads; this condition is declared invalid (fasid). Consequently, no one could demand that of Ali (radiyallahu anhu), nor was it obligatory to kill the rebels. The passage then explains that even those who hold the rebel accountable only require retribution when the rebels' power is broken and no fear of fitna remains. Since those conditions were absent – their power was strong and determination firm – policy demanded overlooking their actions. Moreover, according to the stronger opinion, they bore no liability. Therefore, Ali's caliphate is correct, and he is not to be blamed for not pursuing Uthman's killers.

Key Terms
الباغي
الإمام
الاجتهادان
الاشتراط
الخلافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the condition on the Imam to act according to one of two ijtihads is valid and binding.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows it is necessarily invalid (فاسد) because it is impossible for the Imam to act according to one ijtihad without regard to circumstances, and therefore no one could demand that from Ali.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi say about the condition (al-ishrat) that the Imam should follow one of the two ijtihads regarding the rebels?
A) It is valid and must be implemented.
B) It is invalid because it is not possible for the Imam to act according to one of the two ijtihads necessarily.
C) It is valid only if the rebels submit.
D) It is required by the stronger opinion.

Key Takeaway
Ali's caliphate is correct, and he is not to be blamed for refraining from pursuing the killers of Uthman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone who has wronged you submits and seeks reconciliation, do you truly release them from the past, or do you hold onto resentment? The default ruling in this lesson suggests that submission removes liability. Can you apply that in a relationship where you have been hurt?
?
Ali, despite being the rightful caliph, did not demand immediate retribution for the murder of Uthman because it would have caused fitna. Think of a time when you demanded justice quickly. Did you consider the broader harm your demand might cause? What would it take for you to choose restraint when the cost of justice is more chaos?
?
This lesson argues that the condition upon the Imam to follow one specific ijtihad is invalid. In your own life, are you insisting that others follow your exact method of doing things, even when circumstances change? How might this lesson free you to be more flexible while still upholding truth?

The Ijtihad and Regret of Talha and al-Zubair
Al-Nasafi explains the ijtihad of Talha and al-Zubair and their subsequent regret.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Nasafi, Talha and al-Zubair's actions in the Battle of the Camel were wrong but they were excused due to their ijtihad and tawil, and they later regretted.

Core Idea
Ijtihad can excuse even a wrong action if the person is qualified and sincerely interprets.

In this lesson, we examine al-Nasafi's treatment of a sensitive historical event: the role of Talha and al-Zubair in the Battle of the Camel. The author defends them from being sinful by explaining they acted out of ijtihad, and even though their interpretation was invalid, they are not held accountable. This passage also recounts their sincere regret, showing their ultimate rectitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation to understand the overall narrative of the Battle of the Camel and the positions of Talha, al-Zubair, and Aisha.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice al-Nasafi's opening statement: their action was wrong (khat'a) but done out of ijtihad. They were qualified mujtahids.
Contrast this with the apparent evidence that seemed to justify action.
3
Understand the role of tawil
Even though their interpretation (tawil) was considered invalid, it nullifies accountability. This is a subtle theological point.
The author says 'it is a hidden knowledge that Ali approached and missed' – the exact meaning is debated.
4
Observe the regret
The passage then reports that al-Zubair, Talha, and Aisha all regretted. Aisha wept and wished she had not participated. Talha pledged allegiance to Ali while dying.
This shows their eventual clarity and commitment to the right.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this illustrates a broader kalam principle: a mujtahid who errs is not sinful if they have a valid (even if ultimately incorrect) interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما أمر طلحة والزبير فقد كان خطأ عندنا غير أنهما فعلا ما فعلا عن اجتهاد، وهما كانا من أهل الاجتهاد إذ ظاهر الدلائل يوجب القصاص على قاتل العمد واستئصال شأفة من قصد سلطان الله تعالى بالتوهين ودم إمام المسلمين بالإراقة، فبينا الأمر على هذا الظاهر، فأما الوقوف على إلحاق التأويل وإن كان يعد فاسدًا فالصحيح في حقه إبطال المؤاخذة بما يؤشر به، فهو علم خفي قاربه علي الله [؟]، وحرماه، ولكن لم يخرج فعلهما بذلك عن حد الاجتهاد فكانا مجتهدين أخطأ في اجتهادهما ثم لاح لهما الأمر بعد ذلك، فانحازا عن المركز، وندم الزبير على ذلك، وكذا طلحة، وكذا عائشة - رضي الله عنهما ندمت على ذلك، وكانت تبكي حتى تبلل خمارها وكانت تقول: وددت لو كان لي عشرون ولدًا من رسول الله كلهم مثل عبد الرحمن بن عتاب بن أسيد وأنني ثكلتهم، ولم يكن مني ما كان يوم الجمل - وروى طلحة قال لشاب من عسكر علي وهو يجود: امدد يدك أبايعك لأمير المؤمنين - أراد - والله أعلم -: أن يموت وهو في بيعة إمام عادل.
As for the matter of Talha and al-Zubair, it was wrong according to us, but they did what they did out of ijtihad, and they were among the people of ijtihad, since the apparent evidences (zahir al-dala'il) require retaliation (al-qisas) against the premeditated murderer and eradication of the root of whoever intends to weaken the Sultan of Allah, the Exalted, and to shed the blood of the Imam of the Muslims. So the matter was upon this apparent [ruling]. As for the matter of attaching the interpretation (al-ta'wil) even if it is considered invalid, the correct with respect to it is nullifying the taking to task for what is indicated by it. It is a hidden knowledge that Ali [?] approached and missed? but their action did not depart thereby from the limit of ijtihad, so they were mujtahids who erred in their ijtihad. Then the matter became clear to them after that, so they withdrew from their position. al-Zubair regretted that, and likewise Talha, and likewise Aisha – may Allah be pleased with them – she regretted that, and she used to weep until her veil became wet, and she said: 'I wish I had twenty sons from the Messenger of Allah all like Abdul Rahman bin Attab bin Usayd, and that I had lost them, and that what happened on the Day of the Camel had not happened from me.' And it is narrated that Talha said to a youth from Ali's army while he was dying: 'Extend your hand, I pledge allegiance to you to the Commander of the Faithful' – meaning, Allah knows best – that he should die while in the allegiance of a just Imam.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage discusses the actions of Talha and al-Zubair in the Battle of the Camel. The author states that their action was wrong (khat'a) but they acted out of ijtihad, as they were qualified for ijtihad. The apparent evidence (zahir al-dala'il) required retaliation (qisas) against the premeditated murderer and eradication of those who intended to weaken the Sultan of Allah and shed the blood of the Imam of the Muslims. So this was the apparent ruling. However, they had an interpretation (ta'wil) that, although considered invalid, invalidates taking them to task for what they did. Their action did not exit the bounds of ijtihad; they were mujtahids who erred. Later they realized the truth and withdrew from their position. The passage then reports their remorse: al-Zubair, Talha, and Aisha all regretted. Aisha wept and wished she had not participated. Talha, while dying, pledged allegiance to Ali by extending his hand to a youth, wanting to die under a just Imam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Talha and al-Zubair were sinful rebels and thus not among the rightly guided companions.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that although their action was wrong, they acted out of qualified ijtihad and had a tawil (interpretation) that, though invalid, prevents taking them to task. They later regretted and were thus excused.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why did Talha and al-Zubair's action not make them sinful despite being wrong?
A) Because they repented immediately.
B) Because they acted out of ijtihad and had a tawil that, although invalid, prevents accountability.
C) Because they were companions and immune from error.
D) Because they were forced.

Key Takeaway
Talha and al-Zubair erred in their ijtihad in the Battle of the Camel but were excused due to their ijtihad and later regretted, affirming their status as righteous companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look back at your life, are there decisions you made with sincere ijtihad that later proved wrong? What stops you from admitting regret and correcting course, as Talha and al-Zubair did?
?
The companions wept and wished they could undo their actions. Is there anything in your past that you sincerely regret but have not yet fully turned away from? What would true repentance look like for you today?
?
Talha, while dying, wanted to be under the allegiance of a just Imam. Do you prioritize the unity and justice of the Muslim community over your own ego and past decisions?

Refutation of Absolute Taswib and al-Maturidi's Principle on Tawil
Al-Nasafi refutes absolute taswib and quotes al-Maturidi on tawil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on ijtihad error among the Companions and how tawil excuses without affirming all interpretations as correct.

Core Idea
Erroneous tawil can excuse, but not all mujtahidun are correct.

The Battle of the Camel raised a theological dilemma: were all participants correct in their ijtihad? Some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith claimed yes. Al-Nasafi disagrees, arguing that only Ali was correct, yet the others are excused through tawil. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and quotes al-Maturidi's key principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the contrast between the mutakallimun's view and al-Nasafi's position.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إلا أنهم لم يبلغوا في خطئهم مبلغ الفسق'.
2
Identify the two positions
The mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith held that every mujtahid is correct (musib). Al-Nasafi rejects this: only Ali was correct, but the others are not sinful.
3
Examine al-Maturidi's principle
Al-Maturidi's statement explains why the erroneous mujtahid is excused: tawil makes what he sees as true 'like something commanded', and this ignorance (jahala) is more effective in removing obligation than ignorance of creation or forgetfulness.
The term 'التأويل' (tawil) is key—interpretation based on valid reasoning.
4
Reflect on the implication
This principle preserves the high status of the Companions while affirming that only Ali's ijtihad was correct. Error in ijtihad does not automatically entail sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن بعض متكلمي أهل الحديث كان يقول : كل ما كان منهم كان مبنيًا على الاجتهاد وكل مصيب، فكان على رأي هذا كلهم مصيبون إذ كان من مذهبه أن كل مجتهد في فروع الدين مصيب، وعندنا وإن لم يكن كذلك، وكان علي هو المصيب دون غيرهم إلا أنهم لم يبلغوا في خطئهم مبلغ الفسق، ولهذا قال شيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي رحمه الله - الأصل أن التأويل لأهله يجعله لما يرى عنده أنه حق كالمأمور به فهو أعظم من العذر، وأبلغ في معنى الجهالة التي تسقط الكلفة من الجهالة بأصل الخلقة والنشوء أو بالخطأ الذي اعتراه والسهو
Moreover, some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith used to say: 'Everything that came from them was based on ijtihad, and everyone is correct (musib).' So according to this view, they are all correct, since his school is that every mujtahid in the branches of religion is correct. According to us, although it is not so, and Ali was the correct one to the exclusion of others, they did not reach in their error the level of fisq (immorality). For this reason, our teacher Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The principle is that tawil for its people makes what he sees as true like something commanded; it is greater than an excuse and more effective in the sense of ignorance (al-jahala) that removes obligation than ignorance of the origin of creation and growth, or the error that afflicts him and forgetfulness.'
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological principle of ijtihad concerning the Companions in the Battle of the Camel. Some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith held that all actions of the participants were based on ijtihad and that every mujtahid is correct (musib). According to this view, all were correct. However, the author disagrees: Ali was correct, not the others, but they did not reach the level of fisq (immorality) in their error. To support this, he quotes his teacher Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: 'The principle is that tawil for its people makes what they see as true like something commanded; it is greater than an excuse and more effective in the sense of ignorance (jahala) that removes obligation than ignorance of the origin of creation or the error and forgetfulness that afflicts a person.' This means that their tawil, while erroneous, excused them from sin and did not make them fasiq.

Key Terms
مجتهد
مصيب
فسق
التأويل
الجهالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nasafi agrees with the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith that all mujtahidun are correct.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly states 'وعندنا وإن لم يكن كذلك' (according to us it is not so) and says Ali was the only correct one.

Checkpoint
What is the position of al-Nasafi and his teacher al-Maturidi regarding the participants in the Battle of the Camel who opposed Ali?
A) All mujtahidun are correct (musib).
B) Ali was correct, and the others were mistaken but did not reach the level of fisq.
C) They were all wrong and sinful.
D) Their tawil made them fasiq.

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, erroneous ijtihad is not sinful but is excused through tawil, preserving the unity of the Companions without affirming all positions as correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you misinterpret a command of Allah and later discover your error, do you automatically excuse yourself because you thought you were right? How does al-Maturidi's principle—that tawil is 'greater than an excuse'—challenge you to examine whether your ignorance is genuine or convenient?
?
In conflicts where you have taken a side, have you ever considered the possibility that your side might be wrong? If only Ali was correct in that battle, what does that imply about your own willingness to admit error when your loyalty is at stake?
?
How often do you justify your wrong actions by saying 'I didn't know' or 'I was trying my best'? Would you be content to stand before Allah with that excuse when the truth was clear? What would it take for you to seek certainty rather than settle for your own flawed tawil?

The True Story of the Battle of the Camel
Nasafi narrates the Battle of the Camel with an exonerating explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the narration of the Battle of the Camel according to Nasafi and al-Baqillani, and why it was not a deliberate war between the Companions.

Core Idea
The Battle of the Camel was a tragic accident instigated by the enemies of Islam.

This lesson presents the historical account of the Battle of the Camel as related by Nasafi, drawing on the authority of Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. The account emphasizes that the war was not intended by the Companions but was provoked by the assassins of Uthman, who sought to create confusion. Understanding this helps us avoid blaming either side.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key figures: Aisha, Ali, Talha, and Zubair.
The narration comes from al-Baqillani, a renowned Ash'ari theologian.
2
Identify the sequence of events
Trace the events: Aisha's intention to reconcile, the peace agreement, the intervention of Uthman's assassins, the outbreak of war.
3
Understand the role of the assassins
The assassins of Uthman instigated the war by spreading rumors of betrayal in both camps, leading each side to believe the other had broken the peace.
This is the key point that removes blame from the Companions.
4
Note the conclusion
Al-Baqillani concludes that both parties were defending themselves, so blame is removed from them.
This sets the stage for the theological arguments that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روی أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها - لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفيتن فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة. وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني : هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة الرفيعة، فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب منهم ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تُزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له
And it is narrated that Aisha - may Allah be pleased with her and her father - did not fight Ali, nor did Ali fight her. Rather, Aisha intended to reconcile between the two parties, but war occurred between them. Then Ali honored Aisha and returned her to Medina, honored and protected. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the theologians of the people of hadith, narrated from some eminent scholars: that the incident between them was not based on a resolve for war, but rather it was sudden, and each of the two parties was defending themselves, thinking that the other party had betrayed them, because matters had been settled among them, peace was concluded, and they had dispersed in mutual satisfaction. Then the murderers of Uthman feared being overpowered and surrounded, so they gathered and consulted, disagreed, then agreed to form two groups and start war in the two camps. They mingled, and the group in Ali's camp shouted, 'Talha and Zubair have betrayed,' and the other group shouted, 'Ali has betrayed.' So they succeeded in that, and war broke out. Each party was defending itself, and this was correct from both parties. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view, and with this opinion blame is removed from both parties. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was at the high rank she was. So whoever stretches his tongue criticizing them is the one criticized in his religion, judged to be in misguidance and innovation - may Allah protect us from that. And by encompassing this summary, we recognize the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in their suspension of judgment regarding them, and their saying: 'We do not know who is right and who is wrong among them.' And the error of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is right and the other is wrong.' And we align ourselves with each party individually because their justice is established by consensus, and it is not removed by disagreement. And this, along with the corruption in it, regarding suspension of judgment in the matter of Ali despite the appearance of evidence of his correctness, is extremely corrupt. For to take one of the two persons as a friend individually while knowing that one of them is undeserving of that is invalid, because it entails befriending an enemy of Allah, according to them, with certainty. Then we say: It is incumbent upon you, by analogy with your statement, that if there were two women, one of whom you love but you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one of them individually. Likewise, regarding the grave of a prophet and the grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each specific one, it would be incumbent upon you to affirm the prophethood of each individually, which is disbelief. And also by this is shown the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya, when he claimed concerning Ali, Talha, and Zubair that they were hypocrites and polytheists, yet they are among the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, concerning the people of Badr: 'Perhaps Allah the Exalted looked upon them and said: Do as you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And whoever judges these eminent ones as hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a theological-historical argument about the Battle of the Camel and the proper stance of a Sunni Muslim towards the Companions involved. The author begins by reporting a narration that Aisha did not intend to fight Ali, but rather to reconcile, and the war broke out unexpectedly. He then cites Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's account that the battle was not premeditated but was instigated by the assassins of Uthman to create chaos; each party believed they were defending themselves, so both were correct in their action. After presenting this explanatory narration, the author asserts that regardless of the details, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair are among the ten promised Paradise, and Aisha holds a high rank. Therefore, anyone who speaks ill of them is himself misguided and an innovator. The author then critiques several erroneous positions: (1) The tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata (founders of the Mu'tazila) who claimed ignorance of who was right or wrong; (2) The view of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl (also Mu'tazila) that one party must be right and the other wrong, without specification. The author argues that the correct view is to love all participants individually, based on the established justice of the Companions, which is not invalidated by their differences. He then presents a reductio ad absurdum: If one must love only one of two persons without knowing which is the true believer, one would have to apply the same logic to marrying one of two women or identifying a prophet's grave—leading to absurd or blasphemous conclusions. Finally, he condemns the extreme position of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih of the Bakriyya sect, who claimed that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were hypocrites and polytheists but still forgiven due to the hadith of Badr. The author highlights the contradiction in declaring someone a polytheist and then claiming their forgiveness.

Key Terms
عائشة
علي
طلحة
الزبير
الباقلاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Battle of the Camel was a planned war of aggression by Aisha against Ali.
Correction: The passage shows that Aisha intended reconciliation, and the war was triggered by the assassins of Uthman, not by any Companion.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's account, who instigated the Battle of the Camel?
A) Aisha
B) Ali
C) The assassins of Uthman
D) Talha and Zubair

Key Takeaway
The Battle of the Camel was not a planned conflict between the Companions; it was a tragic accident caused by the enemies of Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a story that puts someone you respect in a negative light, do you immediately accept it or do you pause to consider that there might be deception and false rumors at play? How can you train yourself to be more careful like the Companions who were tricked?
?
The murderers of Uthman succeeded because they exploited trust and spread lies. Are there relationships in your life where whispers or gossip have caused you to distance yourself from someone? What step will you take today to repair that bond and verify the truth?
?
If the Companions, the best of people, could be deceived into fighting each other despite pure intentions, how much more careful must you be about your assumptions towards fellow Muslims? What specific habit will you adopt to avoid acting on suspicion?

The High Status of the Companions Involved
Nasafi affirms the high rank of the Companions and condemns their critics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Companions involved in the Battle of the Camel are beyond reproach and why criticizing them is a sign of misguidance.

Core Idea
Ali, Talha, Zubair, and Aisha are guaranteed Paradise; criticizing them is forbidden.

After recounting the events of the Battle of the Camel, Nasafi asserts the high spiritual rank of the key figures involved. He reminds us that Ali, Talha, and Zubair are among the ten Companions promised Paradise, and Aisha holds a special status. Therefore, anyone who speaks ill of them is in fact harming his own faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the assertion
Read the passage where Nasafi states that regardless of the details, we know the high status of these Companions.
2
Identify the basis of their status
They are among the ten promised Paradise, and Aisha is the Mother of the Believers with a high rank.
This is based on clear hadith and consensus.
3
Understand the consequence
Whoever criticizes them is himself criticized, judged to be in misguidance and innovation.
This is a strong warning against defaming the Companions.
4
Reflect on the principle
The lesson teaches that our stance towards the Companions must be one of love and respect, not criticism.

وقد روی أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها - لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفيتن فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة. وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني : هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة الرفيعة، فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب منهم ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تُزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له
And it is narrated that Aisha - may Allah be pleased with her and her father - did not fight Ali, nor did Ali fight her. Rather, Aisha intended to reconcile between the two parties, but war occurred between them. Then Ali honored Aisha and returned her to Medina, honored and protected. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the theologians of the people of hadith, narrated from some eminent scholars: that the incident between them was not based on a resolve for war, but rather it was sudden, and each of the two parties was defending themselves, thinking that the other party had betrayed them, because matters had been settled among them, peace was concluded, and they had dispersed in mutual satisfaction. Then the murderers of Uthman feared being overpowered and surrounded, so they gathered and consulted, disagreed, then agreed to form two groups and start war in the two camps. They mingled, and the group in Ali's camp shouted, 'Talha and Zubair have betrayed,' and the other group shouted, 'Ali has betrayed.' So they succeeded in that, and war broke out. Each party was defending itself, and this was correct from both parties. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view, and with this opinion blame is removed from both parties. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was at the high rank she was. So whoever stretches his tongue criticizing them is the one criticized in his religion, judged to be in misguidance and innovation - may Allah protect us from that. And by encompassing this summary, we recognize the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in their suspension of judgment regarding them, and their saying: 'We do not know who is right and who is wrong among them.' And the error of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is right and the other is wrong.' And we align ourselves with each party individually because their justice is established by consensus, and it is not removed by disagreement. And this, along with the corruption in it, regarding suspension of judgment in the matter of Ali despite the appearance of evidence of his correctness, is extremely corrupt. For to take one of the two persons as a friend individually while knowing that one of them is undeserving of that is invalid, because it entails befriending an enemy of Allah, according to them, with certainty. Then we say: It is incumbent upon you, by analogy with your statement, that if there were two women, one of whom you love but you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one of them individually. Likewise, regarding the grave of a prophet and the grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each specific one, it would be incumbent upon you to affirm the prophethood of each individually, which is disbelief. And also by this is shown the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya, when he claimed concerning Ali, Talha, and Zubair that they were hypocrites and polytheists, yet they are among the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, concerning the people of Badr: 'Perhaps Allah the Exalted looked upon them and said: Do as you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And whoever judges these eminent ones as hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a theological-historical argument about the Battle of the Camel and the proper stance of a Sunni Muslim towards the Companions involved. The author begins by reporting a narration that Aisha did not intend to fight Ali, but rather to reconcile, and the war broke out unexpectedly. He then cites Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's account that the battle was not premeditated but was instigated by the assassins of Uthman to create chaos; each party believed they were defending themselves, so both were correct in their action. After presenting this explanatory narration, the author asserts that regardless of the details, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair are among the ten promised Paradise, and Aisha holds a high rank. Therefore, anyone who speaks ill of them is himself misguided and an innovator. The author then critiques several erroneous positions: (1) The tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata (founders of the Mu'tazila) who claimed ignorance of who was right or wrong; (2) The view of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl (also Mu'tazila) that one party must be right and the other wrong, without specification. The author argues that the correct view is to love all participants individually, based on the established justice of the Companions, which is not invalidated by their differences. He then presents a reductio ad absurdum: If one must love only one of two persons without knowing which is the true believer, one would have to apply the same logic to marrying one of two women or identifying a prophet's grave—leading to absurd or blasphemous conclusions. Finally, he condemns the extreme position of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih of the Bakriyya sect, who claimed that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were hypocrites and polytheists but still forgiven due to the hadith of Badr. The author highlights the contradiction in declaring someone a polytheist and then claiming their forgiveness.

Key Terms
العشرة المبشرون بالجنة
عائشة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of Ali, Talha, and Zubair?
A) They were righteous but not guaranteed Paradise
B) They are among the ten promised Paradise
C) They were misguided but later forgiven
D) Their status is unknown

Key Takeaway
Criticizing the Companions who were promised Paradise is a sign of misguidance and innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone speak critically about any Companion, what is your immediate reaction? Does this passage force you to examine whether your silence or agreement is a sign of misguidance in your own heart?
?
If you truly believed that criticizing these Companions is a sign of innovation and misguidance, what would you have to change about the conversations you tolerate or participate in?
?
Are there any doubts in your mind about the status of Aisha, Talha, or Zubair that you have not confronted? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to the revealed rank of these Companions even if your reasoning objects?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of Mu'tazilite Stances on the Companions
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite positions of tawaqquf and forced differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite positions of suspending judgment (tawaqquf) or asserting that one party was right and the other wrong are both incorrect.

Core Idea
Neither suspension of judgment nor forced partisanship is correct; we love all Companions equally.

Nasafi now turns to refute two erroneous theological positions that emerged regarding the Battle of the Camel. The first is the tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who said they do not know who was right. The second is the view of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl that one party must be right and the other wrong. Nasafi argues that both are wrong because the Companions are all just and their disagreements do not invalidate their status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two refuted positions
Read the passage and note the two groups: those who suspend judgment and those who insist one is right and the other wrong.
2
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi argues that we align ourselves with each party individually because their justice is established by consensus and not removed by disagreement.
This is a key principle: all Companions are trustworthy and honored.
3
Analyze the inconsistency
Suspending judgment about Ali is particularly corrupt because evidence shows he was correct. Insisting one is right without specification leads to the absurdity of potentially befriending an enemy of Allah.
The argument uses logic to show both positions are untenable.
4
Apply the principle
The correct stance is to love and respect all the Companions involved, without choosing sides.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to suspend judgment about the Companions of the Battle of the Camel.
Correction: The passage shows that the Companions are all just by consensus, so we must affirm their righteousness and not suspend judgment.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the main problem with the position that one party was right and the other wrong without specifying?
A) It is too vague
B) It leads to the possibility of befriending an enemy of Allah

Key Takeaway
The correct stance is to love all the Companions involved in the Battle of the Camel; neither suspending judgment nor forced partisanship is acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the Companions' disagreements, do you find yourself leaning toward suspending judgment to avoid discomfort, or do you confidently affirm their justice as Nasafi commands? Which position shows a lack of trust in Allah's decree?
?
Nasafi says that choosing one party without certainty is invalid because you might be befriending an enemy of Allah. Have you ever been quick to take sides in a conflict without seeking truth, risking allegiance to what Allah dislikes? How can you change this?
?
The justice of the Companions is established by consensus—do you truly feel love for all of them equally, including those you may have reservations about? What would it take for you to remove any partiality from your heart?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
The Reductio Argument: Absurdity of Choosing Sides
Nasafi uses analogies to demonstrate the absurdity of forcing a choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's logical argument that forcing a choice between the Companions leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If you must choose one side without knowing who is right, you'd have to marry blindly or accept false prophethood.

Nasafi strengthens his refutation with a powerful logical analogy. He argues that if the position of the Mu'tazilites (that one must be right and the other wrong) were correct, then a person would have to apply the same logic to other situations, such as marrying one of two women without knowing which one he loves, or affirming the prophethood of a grave without knowing if it belongs to a prophet or an unbeliever. These consequences are clearly absurd or even blasphemous, thus invalidating the original position.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogies
Read the two examples: marrying one of two women and identifying a prophet's grave.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument uses 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning): if the principle is applied consistently, it leads to absurdity.
This is a common method in kalam to refute invalid positions.
3
See the conclusion
The absurd outcomes show the original premise is false. The correct view is that we do not have to choose; we love all the Companions.
4
Apply the lesson
This teaches that theological principles must be consistent and not lead to impossible or blasphemous conclusions.

Source Text

وقد روی أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها - لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفيتن فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة. وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني : هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة الرفيعة، فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب منهم ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تُزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له
And it is narrated that Aisha - may Allah be pleased with her and her father - did not fight Ali, nor did Ali fight her. Rather, Aisha intended to reconcile between the two parties, but war occurred between them. Then Ali honored Aisha and returned her to Medina, honored and protected. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the theologians of the people of hadith, narrated from some eminent scholars: that the incident between them was not based on a resolve for war, but rather it was sudden, and each of the two parties was defending themselves, thinking that the other party had betrayed them, because matters had been settled among them, peace was concluded, and they had dispersed in mutual satisfaction. Then the murderers of Uthman feared being overpowered and surrounded, so they gathered and consulted, disagreed, then agreed to form two groups and start war in the two camps. They mingled, and the group in Ali's camp shouted, 'Talha and Zubair have betrayed,' and the other group shouted, 'Ali has betrayed.' So they succeeded in that, and war broke out. Each party was defending itself, and this was correct from both parties. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view, and with this opinion blame is removed from both parties. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was at the high rank she was. So whoever stretches his tongue criticizing them is the one criticized in his religion, judged to be in misguidance and innovation - may Allah protect us from that. And by encompassing this summary, we recognize the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in their suspension of judgment regarding them, and their saying: 'We do not know who is right and who is wrong among them.' And the error of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is right and the other is wrong.' And we align ourselves with each party individually because their justice is established by consensus, and it is not removed by disagreement. And this, along with the corruption in it, regarding suspension of judgment in the matter of Ali despite the appearance of evidence of his correctness, is extremely corrupt. For to take one of the two persons as a friend individually while knowing that one of them is undeserving of that is invalid, because it entails befriending an enemy of Allah, according to them, with certainty. Then we say: It is incumbent upon you, by analogy with your statement, that if there were two women, one of whom you love but you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one of them individually. Likewise, regarding the grave of a prophet and the grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each specific one, it would be incumbent upon you to affirm the prophethood of each individually, which is disbelief. And also by this is shown the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya, when he claimed concerning Ali, Talha, and Zubair that they were hypocrites and polytheists, yet they are among the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, concerning the people of Badr: 'Perhaps Allah the Exalted looked upon them and said: Do as you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And whoever judges these eminent ones as hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a theological-historical argument about the Battle of the Camel and the proper stance of a Sunni Muslim towards the Companions involved. The author begins by reporting a narration that Aisha did not intend to fight Ali, but rather to reconcile, and the war broke out unexpectedly. He then cites Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's account that the battle was not premeditated but was instigated by the assassins of Uthman to create chaos; each party believed they were defending themselves, so both were correct in their action. After presenting this explanatory narration, the author asserts that regardless of the details, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair are among the ten promised Paradise, and Aisha holds a high rank. Therefore, anyone who speaks ill of them is himself misguided and an innovator. The author then critiques several erroneous positions: (1) The tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata (founders of the Mu'tazila) who claimed ignorance of who was right or wrong; (2) The view of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl (also Mu'tazila) that one party must be right and the other wrong, without specification. The author argues that the correct view is to love all participants individually, based on the established justice of the Companions, which is not invalidated by their differences. He then presents a reductio ad absurdum: If one must love only one of two persons without knowing which is the true believer, one would have to apply the same logic to marrying one of two women or identifying a prophet's grave—leading to absurd or blasphemous conclusions. Finally, he condemns the extreme position of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih of the Bakriyya sect, who claimed that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were hypocrites and polytheists but still forgiven due to the hadith of Badr. The author highlights the contradiction in declaring someone a polytheist and then claiming their forgiveness.

Key Terms
قياس
أمرأتين
قبر نبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say would be the consequence of applying the Mu'tazilite logic to two women?
A) You would marry the one you love
B) You would have to marry each individually
C) You would abstain from marriage
D) You would ask for a sign

Key Takeaway
Forcing a choice between the Companions leads to logical absurdities, so the correct stance is to love all of them without choosing sides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement among Muslims today, do you feel compelled to pick a side and condemn the other? How does this passage challenge that impulse?
?
Are there people in your life whom you have judged—even secretly—as being on the 'wrong side' without knowing the full truth? How would this analogy of the two graves change your attitude toward them?
?
The analogy of marrying both women shows the absurdity of acting without knowledge. In what area of your faith are you acting on assumptions rather than clear guidance, and what step can you take today to seek the truth instead?

Refutation of the Bakriyya Extremist View
Nasafi refutes the Bakriyya sect's inconsistent claim about the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Bakriyya sect's claim that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were hypocrites yet forgiven is contradictory and false.

Core Idea
Declaring Companions polytheists while claiming they are forgiven is an absurd contradiction.

Nasafi concludes by criticizing an extreme sect known as the Bakriyya, named after Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih. This sect bizarrely claimed that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were hypocrites and polytheists, yet they will be in Paradise because of the hadith about the people of Badr. Nasafi exposes the inherent contradiction: if they are polytheists, how can they be forgiven without repentance? This shows the ignorance of the sect's leader.

Guided Path
1
Read the Bakriyya claim
Read the passage describing the Bakriyya view: they say Ali, Talha, and Zubair are hypocrites and polytheists, but are forgiven and in Paradise.
2
Identify the contradiction
How can someone be a polytheist and still be forgiven without repentance? The hadith of Badr applies to sincere believers, not polytheists.
The hadith about the people of Badr indicates Allah forgave them, but they were not polytheists.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi calls this 'ignorance' and shows that the leader of the Bakriyya is misguided.
4
Apply the lesson
This warns against extreme views that both demonize the Companions and then contradict themselves.

Commentary
This passage is a theological-historical argument about the Battle of the Camel and the proper stance of a Sunni Muslim towards the Companions involved. The author begins by reporting a narration that Aisha did not intend to fight Ali, but rather to reconcile, and the war broke out unexpectedly. He then cites Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's account that the battle was not premeditated but was instigated by the assassins of Uthman to create chaos; each party believed they were defending themselves, so both were correct in their action. After presenting this explanatory narration, the author asserts that regardless of the details, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubair are among the ten promised Paradise, and Aisha holds a high rank. Therefore, anyone who speaks ill of them is himself misguided and an innovator. The author then critiques several erroneous positions: (1) The tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata (founders of the Mu'tazila) who claimed ignorance of who was right or wrong; (2) The view of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl (also Mu'tazila) that one party must be right and the other wrong, without specification. The author argues that the correct view is to love all participants individually, based on the established justice of the Companions, which is not invalidated by their differences. He then presents a reductio ad absurdum: If one must love only one of two persons without knowing which is the true believer, one would have to apply the same logic to marrying one of two women or identifying a prophet's grave—leading to absurd or blasphemous conclusions. Finally, he condemns the extreme position of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih of the Bakriyya sect, who claimed that Ali, Talha, and Zubair were hypocrites and polytheists but still forgiven due to the hadith of Badr. The author highlights the contradiction in declaring someone a polytheist and then claiming their forgiveness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions involved in the Battle of the Camel were hypocrites but still forgiven.
Correction: The passage shows this is a contradiction; Companions who are polytheists cannot be magically forgiven. The hadith of Badr applies to sincere believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Sects and Disagreements

Refutation of Tawaqquf and the Claim of Unknown Righteousness Among the Companions
Nasafi refutes two Mu'tazili positions on the Companions' conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi refutes both the Mu'tazili position of suspending judgment and the claim of unknown righteousness regarding the conflict among the Companions, and how his analogies expose the absurdity of these positions.

Core Idea
Neutrality on the Companions' conflict is not safe; it leads to absurdities that imply disbelief.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles two Mu'tazili positions that attempt to navigate the conflict between Ali and his opponents. One group claimed we cannot know who was right, while another claimed we know one was right but not which. Nasafi argues that both positions are invalid because the Companions' probity is established by consensus. He then demonstrates the foolishness of these views through striking analogies that show how treating two opposites equally when truth is evident leads to impossible consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two errors
Read the passage and find the two Mu'tazili positions that Nasafi refutes. Note the names of the individuals associated with each position.
One position is tawaqquf, the other is the claim of unknown righteousness.
2
Understand the principle
Notice how Nasafi uses the established probity (adala) of all Companions as a basis to reject both positions. Why does consensus on their probity make these positions invalid?
3
Analyze the analogies
Examine the analogies of the two women and the two graves. Reflect on how they expose the absurdity of the Mu'tazili claims and what they imply about treating truth and falsehood equally.
Consider how the analogies lead to impossible or disbelieving conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرفيعة، فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب منهم ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تُزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر
... among the exalted matters: whoever extends his tongue against them (the Companions) in slander is himself slandered in his religion and judged as being in misguidance and innovation – may Allah the Exalted protect us from that. And by grasping this summary we recognize the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in their suspension of judgment concerning them (the conflicting parties) and their statement: 'We do not know who among them is correct and who is mistaken.' And the error of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl in their statement: 'We know that one of them is correct and the other mistaken.' And we affirm loyalty to each of the two parties individually because their probity is established by consensus and is not removed by disagreement. This, despite its corruption, regarding the suspension concerning Ali with the appearance of proofs of his correctness is extremely corrupt, because showing loyalty to one of the two persons individually while knowing that one of them does not deserve that is invalid, as it entails loyalty to an enemy of Allah according to them with certainty. Then we say: On the basis of your statement, it would be incumbent upon you that if there are two women, one of whom is more beloved to you and you do not know her specifically, you should marry each of them individually. Likewise, in the case of a grave of a prophet and a grave of a disbeliever, if you do not know each one specifically, you are required to affirm prophethood for each one individually, and that is disbelief.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage refutes two erroneous positions regarding the conflict between Ali and his opponents among the Companions. First, Nasafi condemns those who slander the Companions as misguided. Then he identifies the first error: the tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who claim ignorance of which party is right. The second error: the claim of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl that we know one party is right but not which. Nasafi argues that both are incorrect because the probity of all the Companions is established by consensus and cannot be negated by this disagreement. He then points out the particular corruption of suspending judgment about Ali, given the clear evidence of his correctness. To demonstrate the absurdity of their positions, he presents two analogies: one concerning marriage to two women without knowing which is beloved, and another concerning affirming prophethood for both a prophet's grave and a disbeliever's grave. Both analogies show that treating two opposites equally when one is correct leads to impossible or disbelieving conclusions.

Key Terms
توقف (tawaqquf)
عدالة (adala)
موالاة (muwalat)
إصابة (isaba)
فساد (fasad)
كفر (kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That suspending judgment on the Companions is a safe or neutral position.
Correction: Nasafi shows that such neutrality is not safe because it implies treating truth and falsehood equally, leading to absurdities like affirming prophethood for a disbeliever's grave.

Checkpoint
What do the analogies of the two women and the two graves illustrate?
A) The Companions are all equally righteous.
B) It is necessary to know which side is correct to avoid error.
C) Both sides should be loved equally.
D) The analogies prove nothing.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes both tawaqquf and the claim of unknown righteousness by demonstrating that they lead to impossible conclusions, affirming that the probity of the Companions and the truth of Ali's position are clear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever remained silent on a matter of truth because it was safer to say 'I don't know'? How does this lesson expose the danger of such silence in areas where Allah has made the truth clear?
?
When faced with a disagreement among those you respect, do you seek to identify the truth or do you hide behind 'both sides have good points'? What is the cost of refusing to take a stand when the truth is evident?
?
The analogy of the two graves forces you to consider: if you were to stand before two graves, one of a prophet and one of a disbeliever, and you could not tell them apart, would you honor them both? Where in your life have you treated truth and falsehood as equal?

Refutation of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih on the Companions of Badr
Nasafi refutes Bakr's contradictory claim about the Companions of Badr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi declares Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih a disbeliever due to his contradictory claim about the Companions of Badr.

Core Idea
Declaring a polytheist forgiven is a self-contradiction that leads to disbelief.

Nasafi continues his refutation of deviant sects by exposing the ignorance of the Bakriyya leader. He shows how Bakr's claim that Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were both polytheists and forgiven by Allah is logically impossible. This passage reveals the boundary of orthodox belief regarding the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the hadith of Badr is used as a proof.
2
Identify the contradiction
Pay attention to the two claims: that these Companions were hypocrites/polytheists AND that they are forgiven and among the people of Paradise. Ask yourself: why is this a contradiction?
In Islam, polytheism (shirk) is the gravest sin and forgivable only through repentance; unconditional forgiveness for a polytheist is impossible.
3
Understand the ruling of kufr
Nasafi says there is no doubt among Muslims that such a person is a kafir. How does he connect the contradiction to disbelief? Reflect on the logic: if someone makes an impossible claim about Allah's forgiveness, they have rejected clear teachings, which is kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: 'لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم' ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له فلا شك لأحد من المسلمين في كفره - عصمنا الله.
And by it there also became apparent the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the head of the Bakriyya, when he claimed regarding Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr that they were hypocrites and polytheists, except that they are among the people of Paradise, due to the saying of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, regarding the people of Badr: 'Perhaps Allah the Exalted looked upon them and said: Do whatever you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And whoever judges these eminent figures as hypocrisy and polytheism, then considers the polytheist forgiven, there is no doubt among any Muslim about his disbelief – may Allah protect us.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage refutes the leader of the Bakriyya sect, Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih. Nasafi identifies his ignorance by pointing out that he claimed Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were hypocrites (munafiqun) and polytheists (mushrikun), yet also asserted they would be among the people of Paradise based on a hadith about the participants in the Battle of Badr. Nasafi declares that anyone who judges these Companions to be hypocrites and polytheists and then considers such a polytheist forgiven is undoubtedly a disbeliever in the consensus of Muslims. The self-contradiction of calling someone a polytheist while affirming forgiveness for him is the basis of this ruling.

Key Terms
منافق (munafiq)
مشرك (mushrik)
كفر (kufr)
جهالة (jahala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can claim that the Companions of Badr were hypocrites and polytheists yet still be forgiven by Allah.
Correction: Nasafi argues that this is a contradiction; polytheism is incompatible with forgiveness without repentance, so the claimant is a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi consider Bakr bin Abd Rabbih a kafir?
A) Because he believed Companions are hypocrites.
B) Because he claimed polytheists can be forgiven.
C) Because he doubted the hadith of Badr.
D) Because he rejected the caliphate of Ali.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi exposes that claiming polytheists are forgiven is a contradiction that leads to disbelief, preserving the absolute distinction between faith and polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that Bakr claimed Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were polytheists yet forgiven. Have you ever held onto a negative judgment of a fellow Muslim while still assuming they are okay with Allah? How does this contradiction reflect a lack of tawheed in your own heart? Commit to examining your judgments in light of clear Islamic principles.
?
The hadith about Badr is a mercy for believers who fought. But Bakr twisted it to apply to people he called polytheists. In your life, have you taken a verse or hadith out of its proper context to justify a preconceived notion? What is one verse you need to re-learn in its proper context today?
?
Nasafi says there is no doubt among Muslims about the kufr of Bakr’s statement. Do you feel the weight of that warning? When you speak about the Companions, do you guard your tongue with the same fear of falling into misguidance? What step will you take today to ensure your speech about the Sahaba is based on love and accuracy, not sectarian bias?

The Battle of Siffin: Ali's Righteousness and Muawiya's Rebellion
Nasafi establishes Ali's correctness at Siffin and discusses Sunni disagreement on labeling Muawiya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi considers Ali to be correct at Siffin, why Muawiya's error does not make him a fasiq, and the Sunni disagreement on labeling him as a baghi.

Core Idea
Ali was right, Muawiya erred but was not sinful due to interpretation.

This passage from al-Tabsira addresses one of the most sensitive events in early Islamic history: the Battle of Siffin. Imam al-Nasafi affirms Ali's righteousness using historical and rational arguments, while also explaining why those who opposed him, including Muawiya, are not to be condemned as sinners or disbelievers. The discussion then turns to a key theological debate among Sunni scholars over whether to label Muawiya as a rebel (baghi).

Guided Path
1
Understand Nasafi's proof for Ali's correctness
Notice that Nasafi uses Umar's inclusion of Ali in the shura as a testimony of his merit, and argues that Ali's caliphate was established before any opposition.
2
Grasp why Muawiya's error does not make him a fasiq
Nasafi distinguishes between being in error and being sinful: Muawiya acted based on ta'wil (interpretation), so he is not a fasiq. This is a key principle in Sunni theology.
3
Explore the Sunni disagreement on the term 'baghi'
The final part shows that Sunni theologians differ on whether to call Muawiya a baghi. Some refrain out of respect, others apply the term based on scriptural evidence. Understand both positions.
The passage cuts off, but the two opinions are clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الكلام في قتال أهل الشام بصفين على هذا فإن عليا الله كان هو المحق المصيب والأمر فيه أظهر، فإن عليا لله كان ممن أدخله عمر في أهل الشورى، وكان ذلك من عمر شهادة أنه أحق بذلك ممن نازعه مع أن المنازعة حدثت بعد انعقاد إمامته وتقرر خلافته، وبيعة غيره وجدت بعد بيعته فلم تكن الثانية منعقدة، ثم لا ارتياب لأحد له من العلم حظ في تفاوت ما بين علي ومعاوية - رضي الله عنهما - في الفضل والعلم والشجاعة والفناء والسابقة في الإسلام، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان خطأ معاوية ظاهرًا إلا أنه فعل ما فعل أيضا عن تأويل فلم يصر به فاسقا على ما قررنا، ثم لا شك أن من حارب عليا له من الصحابة ومن غيرهم على التأويل لم يصر به كافرًا ولا فاسقا ؛ ولهذا قال علي - فهم إخواننا بغوا علينا - وقال لابن طلحة - أنا وأبوك من أهل هذه الآية: ﴿وَنَزَعْنَا مَا فِي صُدُورِهِم مِّنْ غِلٍّ إِخْوَانًا عَلَى سُرُرٍ مُتَقَابِلِينَ﴾ . ثم اختلف متكلمو أهل السنة والجماعة في تسمية من خالف عليا باغيًا، فمنهم من امتنع عن ذلك فلا يجوز إطلاق اسم الباغي على معاوية، ويقول : ليس ذا أخطأ في اجتهاده، ومنهم من يطلق ذلك الاسم ويستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا﴾ الآية، ويقول النبي عليه السلام لعمار: 'تقتلك الفئة الباغية' ويقول علي : - إخواننا بغوا علينا غير أنهم يمتنعون عن...
And likewise the discussion concerning the fighting of the people of Syria at Siffin is based on this: indeed Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was the correct and right party, and the matter concerning it is more apparent. For Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was among those whom Umar included in the consultative council (shura), and that was from Umar a testimony that he was more entitled to that than those who disputed him, given that the dispute occurred after the conclusion of his imamate and the establishment of his caliphate, and the pledge of allegiance to others occurred after his pledge, so the second was not valid. Then there is no doubt for anyone who has a share of knowledge regarding the difference between Ali and Muawiya – may Allah be pleased with both of them – in virtue, knowledge, courage, selflessness, and precedence in Islam. When this is the case, Muawiya's error is apparent, except that he also did what he did due to interpretation (ta'wil), so he did not thereby become a fasiq according to what we have established. Then there is no doubt that whoever fought Ali among the Companions and others on the basis of interpretation did not thereby become a disbeliever nor a fasiq. For this reason Ali said: 'They are our brothers who wronged us,' and he said to Ibn Talha: 'I and your father are among the people of this verse: {And We have removed whatever rancor may be in their hearts, brothers, on couches facing each other.}' Then the theologians of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a differed concerning the designation of those who opposed Ali as rebels (baghi). Some refrained from that and do not permit applying the term 'baghi' to Muawiya, saying: 'He only erred in his ijtihad.' Others apply that term and adduce the verse of the Exalted: {And if two factions among the believers fight...} and the Prophet's saying to Ammar: 'The rebellious faction will kill you,' and Ali's saying: 'Our brothers wronged us,' except that they refrain from...
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage discusses the Battle of Siffin and its aftermath. Nasafi first asserts that Ali was unequivocally the correct party, and this is even more apparent than earlier cases. He provides evidence: Umar's inclusion of Ali in the shura council was a testimony to his entitlement over his opponents. The dispute over caliphate occurred after Ali's legitimate imamate, and any later pledges were invalid. Nasafi then highlights the clear superiority of Ali over Muawiya in virtue, knowledge, courage, selflessness, and precedence in Islam. Given this, Muawiya's error is clear, but he acted based on ta'wil (interpretation), so he does not become a fasiq. Likewise, all those who fought Ali from the Companions and others, if they acted on ta'wil, are not considered disbelievers or fasiqs. Ali himself acknowledged them as 'brothers who wronged us' and referenced the Quranic verse about removal of rancor. Finally, Nasafi notes a difference among Sunni theologians: some refuse to label Muawiya as a 'baghi' (rebel), while others apply the term based on the Quranic verse, the hadith of Ammar, and Ali's statement. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'except that they refrain from...'.

Key Terms
حق (haqq)
إصابة (isaba)
شورى (shura)
إمامة (imama)
فضل (fadl)
تأويل (ta'wil)
فسق (fisq)
بغي (baghi)
اجتهاد (ijtihad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Muawiya's error at Siffin makes him a fasiq or even a kafir.
Correction: Nasafi clearly states that Muawiya acted on ta'wil (interpretation), so he does not become a fasiq. This principle is applied to all who fought Ali on ta'wil.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does Muawiya's error not make him a fasiq?
A) Because he was a Companion.
B) Because he acted based on interpretation (ta'wil).
C) Because he repented later.
D) Because the scholars disagreed.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi affirms Ali's righteousness and explains that opponents who acted on ta'wil, including Muawiya, are not fasiq, though their error is clear; Sunni scholars differ on whether to label Muawiya as a rebel.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone opposes you, do you immediately judge their intention as sinful? This lesson shows that even Muawiya's error was excused due to ta'wil. Can you think of a situation where you assumed the worst of someone's motive? How would this teaching change your reaction? What will you do the next time you are wronged?
?
The passage shows that Ali called his opponents 'brothers who wronged us.' Do you have any relationship where you hold a grudge? Can you see that person as a brother/sister who may have erred but is still a believer? What step will you take today to remove rancor from your heart?
?
Sunni scholars differ on labeling Muawiya, yet they all respect him. This shows humility in differing. How do you react when you learn that scholars legitimately differ on a matter? Do you feel uncertainty or charity? Reflect on your need for certainty versus your trust in Allah's mercy for those who sincerely seek truth. What can you do to cultivate more tolerance for scholarly differences in your own mind?
