Status of Those Who Fought Ali: Disagreement on Labeling as Baghi
Nasafi discusses the status of Siffin participants and disagreement on calling them baghi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced Sunni position on the participants in the Battle of Siffin and the disagreement among scholars over using the term 'baghi' for them.

Core Idea
Even if in error, those who fought Ali acted due to ijtihad, so they are not fasiqs.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi addresses a sensitive historical episode: the conflict between Ali and Muawiya. The author affirms Ali's correctness but clarifies that Muawiya and his supporters acted based on legitimate interpretation (ta'wil), thus remaining within the fold of faith. The lesson then presents an internal Sunni disagreement about whether to call them 'baghi' (rebels).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow and argument.
Notice the author's careful tone and use of evidence.
2
Identify the main claims
Note the author's assertions: Ali was right, Muawiya erred but due to ta'wil, so he did not become a fasiq.
The same applies to all companions who fought Ali.
3
Examine the disagreement on 'baghi'
Identify the two positions: some refuse to call Muawiya baghi; others apply the term but still avoid calling him fasiq.
Both sides agree on the core point of not being fasiq.
4
Connect to key terms
Understand how ta'wil, baghi, fasiq, and ijtihad function in this context.
This illustrates how Sunni theology handles ijtihad in historical conflicts.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this passage exemplifies the Sunni stance of refraining from judging early Muslim figures harshly.
It also shows the limits of applying legal labels like 'baghi' when ijtihad is involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا شك لأحد من المسلمين في كفره - عصمنا الله. وكذا الكلام في قتال أهل الشام بصفين على هذا فإن عليا الله كان هو المحق المصيب والأمر فيه أظهر، فإن عليا لله كان ممن أدخله عمر في أهل الشورى، وكان ذلك من عمر شهادة أنه أحق بذلك ممن نازعه مع أن المنازعة حدثت بعد انعقاد إمامته وتقرر خلافته، وبيعة غيره وجدت بعد بيعته فلم تكن الثانية منعقدة، ثم لا ارتياب لأحد له من العلم حظ في تفاوت ما بين علي ومعاوية - رضي الله عنهما - في الفضل والعلم والشجاعة والفناء والسابقة في الإسلام، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان خطأ معاوية ظاهرًا إلا أنه فعل ما فعل أيضا عن تأويل فلم يصر به فاسقا على ما قررنا، ثم لا شك أن من حارب عليا له من الصحابة ومن غيرهم على التأويل لم يصر به كافرًا ولا فاسقا ؛ ولهذا قال علي - فهم إخواننا بغوا علينا - وقال لابن طلحة - أنا وأبوك من أهل هذه الآية: ﴿وَنَزَعْنَا مَا فِي صُدُورِهِم مِّنْ علي إخواناً عَلَى سُرُرٍ مُتَقَبِلِينَ﴾. ثم اختلف متكلمو أهل السنة والجماعة في تسمية من خالف عليا باغيًا، فمنهم من امتنع عن ذلك فلا يجوز إطلاق اسم الباغي على معاوية، ويقول : ليس ذا أخطأ في اجتهاده، ومنهم من يطلق ذلك الاسم ويستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا﴾ الآية، ويقول النبي عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" ويقول علي : - إخواننا بغوا علينا غير أنهم يمتنعون عن تسميتهم فساقًا لما مر
There is no doubt for any Muslim in his disbelief - may Allah protect us. Likewise the discussion about fighting the people of Syria at Siffin is upon this, for Ali (radiallahu anhu) was the right and correct one, and the matter in it is more apparent. For Ali (radiallahu anhu) was among those whom Umar included in the council of Shura, and that was from Umar a testimony that he was more entitled to it than those who disputed with him, even though the dispute occurred after the conclusion of his imamate and the establishment of his caliphate, and the pledge to others occurred after his pledge, so the second was not concluded. Then there is no doubt for anyone who has a share of knowledge in the difference between Ali and Muawiya (radiallahu anhuma) in merit, knowledge, bravery, sacrifice, and precedence in Islam. And if the matter is like this, then the error of Muawiya was apparent, except that he did what he did also due to ta'wil, so he did not become a fasiq thereby, upon what we have established. Then there is no doubt that those who fought Ali from the companions and others, due to ta'wil, did not become a disbeliever or a fasiq thereby. For this reason Ali said: 'They are our brothers who rebelled against us' and said to Ibn Talha: 'I and your father are among the people of this verse: {And We shall remove from their breasts any rancor, as brothers on couches facing each other}' Then the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah differed regarding naming those who opposed Ali as baghi. Some refrained from that, so it is not permissible to apply the name baghi to Muawiya, and they say: 'This one [i.e., Muawiya] did not err in his ijtihad.' And some apply that name and use as evidence the saying of the Exalted: {And if two factions among the believers fight} the verse, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Ammar: 'You will be killed by the transgressing party', and Ali says: 'Our brothers rebelled against us', but they refrain from calling them fasiqs due to what has passed.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion about the status of those who fought Ali (radiallahu anhu) in the Battle of Siffin, particularly Muawiya and his supporters. The author first asserts that no Muslim doubts the disbelief of the Khawarij (a reference to the previous page). Then he turns to the people of Syria. He emphasizes that Ali was in the right, being among the six appointed by Umar for the shura, indicating his merit. The author acknowledges Muawiya's apparent error but explains it was due to ta'wil (ijtihad based on interpretation), so he did not become a fasiq. The same applies to all companions who fought Ali—they remain believers and not fasiqs. Ali himself called them 'brothers who rebelled against us' and applied a Quranic verse to them. The author then notes a disagreement among Sunni mutakallimun: some refuse to call Muawiya a baghi (rebel), arguing he did not err; others apply the term based on the Quranic verse and hadith, yet still refrain from calling them fasiqs. The passage thus clarifies that even if the term 'baghi' is used, it does not imply sin leading to fasiq, due to the validity of ijtihad.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
باغٍ (baghi)
فاسق (fasiq)
اجتهاد (ijtihad)
أهل السنة والجماعة (Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling Muawiya a 'baghi' makes him a fasiq, implying sin and loss of moral status.
Correction: The author clarifies that due to ta'wil (ijtihad), even if the term 'baghi' is applied, it does not entail being a fasiq; the person remains a believer and not sinful for the error.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did Muawiya not become a fasiq despite his apparent error?
A) Because he repented and sinned.
B) Because he acted based on ta'wil (ijtihad).
C) Because he was a companion and companions cannot sin.
D) Because Ali forgave him.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni consensus holds that participants in the Battle of Siffin remain believers and not fasiqs, with disagreement only over the label 'baghi', which does not imply sin due to ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone over a religious matter, do you automatically assume they are sinful or misguided? How does the lesson's treatment of Muawiya's ijtihad challenge your tendency to judge others' intentions? What concrete step can you take this week to adopt a more charitable attitude towards a brother or sister with whom you differ?
?
Ali called those who fought him 'brothers'. Can you honestly say that you view those who oppose you in Islamic matters as brothers? If not, what deep-seated pride or anger prevents you? What will you do to root it out, starting today?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether to label Muawiya as baghi, but they all preserved brotherhood and did not declare him a fasiq. Is your focus in conflicts with other Muslims more about 'winning the label' or preserving unity? What relationship in your life is suffering because you prioritized being right over being merciful, and how will you repair it immediately?

Inheritance Ruling for a Baghi: Ta'wil and Liability
Hanafi inheritance ruling for a baghi based on ta'wil equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Hanafi jurists treat a baghi's corrupt ta'wil as equivalent to correct ta'wil for legal liability, using inheritance as an example.

Core Idea
Corrupt ta'wil can still excuse legal liability in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling that illustrates a key principle: that a baghi's mistaken interpretation (ta'wil) may absolve them of certain legal consequences. The inheritance case shows how the baghi is not deprived of inheritance despite killing a just person, because their ta'wil is treated like a correct interpretation in nullifying punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage concerning the Hanafi ruling
Focus on the first part: 'And for this reason our imams considered their corrupt ta'wil as equivalent to correct [ta'wil]...' Understand the ruling on inheritance.
2
Understand the principle of ta'wil in law
The key point is that a corrupt ta'wil does not automatically incur punishment. This is because the intention is not malicious.
3
Reflect on why this matters for kalam
In kalam, we evaluate actions based on intention and knowledge. This fiqh ruling shows how a mistaken interpretation can affect legal outcomes, relevant for assessing the status of those who fought Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا ألحق أصحابنا رحمهم الله تأويلهم الفاسد بالصحيح في حق إبطال المؤاخذة، ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد – رحمهم الله -: إن الباغي إذا قتل مورثه العادل لا يحرم من الميراث كما لو قتل العادل مورثه ومن امتنع عن إطلاق اسم الباغي على هؤلاء يؤول قوله عليه السلام الباغي لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية." فيقول: معناه الطائفة الطالبة دم عثمان نه، يقال: بغى إذا طلب، ولا يلتفت إلى إطلاق الروافض اسم الظالم والفاسق وغير ذلك، لما أقمنا من الدلالة على انتفاء هذه السمات عنهم، وكيف يجوز إطلاق هذه الأسامي على من كان ساعيًا في تحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل وطالبًا لما هو الواجب في ظاهر الشريعة من إيجاب القصاص غير ساع في الاستيلاء على الملك والترأس على الخلائق لينازع غيره ويزيل السطانه إلى نفسه، ولهذا امتنع طلحة والزبير عن تقلد الإمامة والدخول في الخلافة بعد مقتل عثمان الله يأبى ذلك أشد الإباء إلى أن تبين له بمشورة أجلاء الصحابة وجه المصلحة في ذلك على ما مر ذكره، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم تكن تسميتهم بالظالم والفاسق جائزة مع ما بينا من ذلك، والله المموفق.
And for this reason our imams (may Allah have mercy on them) considered their corrupt ta'wil as equivalent to correct [ta'wil] in terms of nullifying liability. And for this reason Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: 'If the baghi kills his just inheritor, he is not deprived of inheritance, just as if the just person kills his inheritor.' And those who refrain from applying the name baghi to these people interpret the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding Ammar: 'You will be killed by the transgressing party.' They say: Its meaning is the group seeking the blood of Uthman [؟] ; it is said: bagha means he sought. And no attention is paid to the Rafidis' application of the names oppressor and fasiq and other than that, because we have established the proof that these attributes are negated from them. And how is it permissible to apply these names to one who was striving to achieve truth and abolish falsehood, and demanding what is obligatory in the outward [appearance] of the Sharia from requiring retaliation, not striving for seizure of rule and leadership over creatures to dispute with another and remove his authority to himself? And for this reason Talha and al-Zubayr refrained from assuming the imamate and entering into the caliphate after the murder of Uthman (radiallahu anhu) - he refused that with the strongest refusal until the aspect of benefit became clear to him through consultation of the noble companions, as mentioned earlier. And when the matter is like this, naming them as oppressor and fasiq is not permissible, along with what we have explained, and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage contains two main points: a legal ruling and a refutation of accusations. First, the author explains that the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) treated the corrupt ta'wil of those who fought Ali as equivalent to correct ta'wil in terms of nullifying legal liability (mu'akhadha). This is illustrated by the inheritance ruling: if a baghi kills his just (adel) inheritor, he does not forfeit his inheritance, just as if a just person kills his inheritor (the just person is not deprived of inheritance if he kills his inheritor mistakenly). This shows that the baghi's status does not affect inheritance rights due to ta'wil. Second, the author addresses those who refrain from calling the opponents of Ali 'baghi' altogether, interpreting the hadith 'tugtaluka al-fi'atu al-baghiya' as 'the group seeking the blood of Uthman' (since bagha can mean to seek). The author then strongly refutes the Rafidi practice of labeling these Companions (like Muawiya, Talha, Zubayr) as oppressors or fasiqs. He argues that these Companions were striving for truth, abolishing falsehood, and demanding retaliation for Uthman's murder according to apparent Sharia rules—not seeking power. He cites Talha and Zubayr's initial refusal to assume leadership as evidence of their noble intentions. Thus, such names are impermissible. The passage ends with a prayer for Allah's guidance.

Key Terms
باغٍ (baghi)
عادل (adel)
تأويل (ta'wil)
ميراث (mirath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a baghi who kills a just person is like a murderer and should be deprived of inheritance.
Correction: The Hanafi ruling shows otherwise: due to ta'wil, the baghi is not deprived of inheritance, just as if a just person killed the inheritor.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what happens if a baghi kills his just inheritor?
A) The baghi is deprived of inheritance.
B) The baghi inherits just as if a just person killed the inheritor.
C) The inheritance goes to the state.
D) The baghi is executed.

Key Takeaway
A baghi's corrupt ta'wil can excuse legal liability, as seen in the inheritance ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used a twisted justification (a corrupt ta'wil) to excuse your own wrongdoing? How does the Hanafi ruling—that even flawed reasoning can nullify worldly liability—make you feel about your accountability before Allah, who knows the true intent?
?
If a corrupt ta'wil can spare a rebel from losing inheritance in this world, does that make you more or less vigilant about the excuses you make for your own sins? What would it take for you to abandon all false justifications and submit fully to Allah's command?
?
The Hanafis treat the baghi's corrupt ta'wil as valid for legal purposes. But on the Day of Judgment, will your flawed reasoning excuse you? How does this discrepancy between worldly leniency and divine justice stir your heart to repent and seek sincerity?

Refuting Rafidi Labels against the Companions
Refutation of Rafidi accusations against Companions who fought Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author refutes the Rafidi practice of calling the Companions who fought Ali 'oppressors' or 'fasiqs', based on their intentions and actions.

Core Idea
The Companions who fought Ali were seeking justice, not power, so labels like oppressor are unjustified.

In this lesson, we examine the author's refutation of those who call the Companions (like Talha, Zubayr, Muawiya) oppressors or fasiqs. The author argues that they were striving for truth and demanding retaliation for Uthman, not seeking worldly power. He cites Talha and Zubayr's initial refusal of leadership as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read from 'ومن امتنع عن إطلاق اسم الباغي' to the end. Note the author's arguments.
2
Understand the Rafidi accusation
Rafidis call these Companions oppressors and fasiqs. The author refutes this by showing their noble intentions.
3
Examine the evidence: Talha and Zubayr's refusal of leadership
This shows they were not seeking power. The author argues this proves their righteousness.
4
Reflect on the implications for kalam
This discussion connects to the status of Companions in Islamic theology, specifically the issue of those involved in fitna.

?

?
The Companions Talha and al-Zubayr refused leadership until they were certain of its benefit. Examine your own life: where are you rushing to take a position or authority without first seeking counsel and clarifying your intention? Write one concrete step you will take this week to ensure your motives are pure.
?
This lesson reveals that even those who fought against Ali cannot be labelled oppressors. Is there someone you have been quick to judge as unjust or sinful without knowing their full story? Pray now for Allah to give you the same restraint shown by the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Rules for Engaging Khawarij and Rebels
Enumeration of obligations for dealing with Khawarij and rebels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven obligations that govern Muslim conduct when dealing with internal rebels, from initial dialogue to battlefield ethics.

Core Idea
Even in rebellion, da'wa and ethics must precede the sword.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi outlines the practical rules derived from the conduct of the early Muslim community (particularly Ali ibn Abi Talib) when confronting the Khawarij and other rebels. It emphasizes that the first response is not violence but summoning to dialogue, and that even in battle, strict limits protect the wounded, the fleeing, and women.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the flow of the list come through.
Notice that the passage is one long sentence listing each obligation.
2
Identify the sequence
The rules are ordered: pre-war obligations (summoning, debating, notice, not initiating) and during-war obligations (not pursuing, not killing wounded, not approaching women).
3
Understand each rule
Consider the rationale behind each rule. For example, why give notice before war? Why not pursue fleeing fighters? Why protect women?
These rules reflect the emphasis on justice and avoidance of excess.
4
Reflect on the principle
The overall principle is restraint and prioritization of da'wa over combat. Ask yourself: How does this contrast with modern counterinsurgency?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لولا ما ذكر أحوالهم من الوقوف على ما هو الواجب في معاملة الخوارج، ومن يبتلى بمحاربته من أهل البغي من الابتداء باستدعائهم ومناظرتهم وترك مبادأتهم ، والنبذ إليهم قبل نصب الحرب معهم، والامتناع عن محاربتهم إلى أن يبتدعوا، وترك اتباع مدبرهم، وتدفيف جريحهم، والتعرض لنسوتهم
Then we say: Were it not that we mentioned their conditions regarding knowing what is obligatory in dealing with the Khawarij, and whoever is afflicted with fighting from the people of baghi: [it is] to begin by summoning them and debating them, and refraining from initiating combat against them, and giving notice to them before waging war with them, and refraining from fighting them until they start [fighting], and abandoning pursuing those who flee, and hastening the death of their wounded, and approaching their women.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a concise enumeration of the proper Islamic protocol for dealing with rebellious groups, specifically the Khawarij and people of baghi (rebellion). The author presents these rules as obligations derived from the practice of the early community. The method includes: (1) beginning by summoning them to dialogue and debate (da'wa and munazara), not immediately fighting; (2) refraining from initiating combat against them; (3) giving formal notice (nabdh) before setting up war; (4) not fighting them until they start the fighting (i.e., defensive posture); (5) not pursuing those who flee the battlefield; (6) not hastening the death of their wounded (i.e., not killing the wounded); (7) not approaching their women (i.e., not harming or molesting women). These rules emphasize restraint, da'wa, and ethical warfare even against internal rebels. The author implies that these are well-known obligations based on the conduct of Ali and the early caliphs.

Key Terms
خوارج (Khawarij)
بغي (baghy)
استدعاء (istid'a)
مناظرة (munazara)
نبذ (nabdh)
مدبر (mudbir)
تدفيف (tadfif)
جريح (jarih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rebels should be attacked immediately without prior dialogue or restraint.
Correction: This passage shows that the priority is da'wa and avoiding initiation of combat; only after notice and defense can fighting occur, and even then with strict ethical limits.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the first step in dealing with the Khawarij or rebellious groups?
A) Attack them immediately to prevent escalation.
B) Summon them and debate with them.
C) Kill their leaders first.
D) Take their women as captives.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic method for dealing with rebels prioritizes dialogue, notice, and restraint in battle over immediate violence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone you strongly disagree with—whether in belief or action—what is your first impulse? Is it to debate and convince, or to cut off and confront? This lesson commands you to start with summoning and debate. How will you change your approach starting today?
?
Imagine you are convinced someone is wrong—seriously wrong, like a rebel against truth. This lesson forbids you from initiating combat against them, even if you are in power. What does that reveal about your heart? Can you honestly say you prefer dialogue over victory, or do you secretly wish for the sword to settle things?
?
The wounded and the fleeing are to be left unharmed, and women are not to be touched. Do you treat those weaker or defeated with mercy, or do you take advantage when you have the upper hand? How will you enact this prophetic ethic in your relationships—even with those you consider enemies?
